Ila as Ghaya: Extension vs Exclusion
Explains two functions of 'ila' in setting a limit: extension or exclusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two functions of 'ila' in a legal text—extension (imtidad) and dropping (isqat)—and determine whether the endpoint is included or excluded based on context.

Core Idea
The particle 'ila' either extends a ruling up to but excluding the endpoint, or drops the ruling beyond including the endpoint—context decides which.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the Arabic particle 'ila' (to/until) is used to set a limit (ghaya) for a ruling. However, it can function in two distinct ways: as an extension (imtidad), meaning the ruling applies up to the limit but does not include the limit itself, or as a dropping (isqat), meaning the ruling includes the limit as part of its scope. This passage from Usul al-Shashi clarifies this crucial distinction with concrete examples from sale and option contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possible meanings of 'ila'
Recognize that 'ila' can convey imtidad (extension) or isqat (dropping). The text introduces these two functions explicitly.
2
Determine the ruling on the endpoint
For imtidad, the endpoint is not included in the ruling (ghaya ghayr dakhila). For isqat, the endpoint is included (ghaya dakhila).
3
Apply to the first example (sale of place up to wall)
The wall is the endpoint; 'ila' functions as imtidad, so the wall is not part of the sale.
Example: 'I bought this place up to this wall' – the wall is excluded.
4
Apply to the second example (sale with option until three days)
The third day is the endpoint; 'ila' functions as isqat, so the third day is part of the option period.
Example: 'He sold with an option until three [days]' – the third day is included.
5
Generalize to other legal texts
This distinction applies to Quranic verses, hadith, contracts, and any text using 'ila' to set a limit.
Always examine context to determine whether 'ila' functions as imtidad or isqat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل إلى لانتهاء الغاية ثم هو في بعض الصور يفيد معنى امتداد الحكم وفي بعض الصوّر يفيد معنى الإسقاط، فإن أفاد الامتداد لا تدخل الغاية في حكم المغيا وإن أفاد الإسقاط تدخل. نظير الأوّل: اشتريت هذا المكان إلى هذا الحائط، لا يدخل الحائط في البيع. ونظير الثاني: باع بشرط الخيار إلى ثلاث، فيدخل الثلاث في الخيار.
Section on 'ila' for the end of the end-point. Then it sometimes conveys the meaning of extending the ruling and sometimes conveys the meaning of dropping. If it conveys extension, the end-point does not enter into the ruling of the limited thing; if it conveys dropping, it does enter. An example of the first: I bought this place up to this wall – the wall does not enter into the sale. An example of the second: He sold with an option until three [days] – the three enters into the option.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage introduces a key distinction in Hanafi usul regarding the particle 'ila' (to/until) when used to indicate an endpoint (ghaya). 'Ila' has two possible meanings: (1) امتداد الحكم (extension of the ruling), where the ruling applies up to but does not include the endpoint; (2) إسقاط (dropping/exclusion), where the ruling includes the endpoint. The first is illustrated with a purchase 'up to this wall': the wall itself is not part of the sale because 'ila' here extends the sale's scope up to the wall, excluding it. The second is illustrated with a sale with an option 'until three days': the third day is included in the option period because 'ila' here drops the ruling beyond the endpoint, thus including the endpoint itself. This distinction is crucial for interpreting legal texts that use 'ila', such as Quranic verses and contractual terms.

Key Terms
إلى (ila): A particle indicating the end-point (ghaya) of a ruling; it can either extend the ruling to the end-point (امتداد) or drop it (إسقاط).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'I bought this place up to this wall', does the wall become part of the sale?
A) Yes, because 'ila' indicates inclusion.
B) No, because 'ila' indicates extension, so the wall is excluded.
C) Yes, because the wall is a boundary.
D) It depends on the price.

Key Takeaway
Whether 'ila' extends or drops depends on the intended meaning determined by context; extension excludes the endpoint, dropping includes it.

The Particle 'Ila' and Its Legal Functions in Hanafi Usul
Analysis of the Arabic particle 'ila' and its legal implications in Hanafi fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the functions of the particle 'ila' (imtidad, isqat, ta'khir) and apply the Hanafi rule to determine whether the limit is included in the ruling.

Core Idea
The particle 'ila' either extends the ruling up to (excluding) or drops the ruling beyond (including) the limit — context decides.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi dissects the Arabic preposition 'ila' when used to specify a limit (ghaya). Understanding its dual function is crucial for interpreting Quranic verses, hadith, and contractual oaths correctly. The Hanafi school distinguishes between imtidad (extension) and isqat (dropping), which determines whether the limit itself is included in the ruling. We will walk through the examples and the intra-madhhab dispute with Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two primary functions of 'ila'
Recognize that 'ila' can indicate either imtidad (extension of the ruling up to the limit, where the limit is excluded) or isqat (dropping of the ruling beyond the limit, where the limit is included). Also note a third function: ta'khir (postponement of the ruling).
The context of the text determines which function is intended.
2
Distinguish imtidad from isqat using examples
Example of imtidad: 'I bought this place up to this wall' – the wall is not included in the sale. Example of isqat: 'Sold with option up to three days' – the third day is included. The key is whether without 'ila' the ruling would naturally include the limit.
If the initial part alone would cover the limit, 'ila' likely indicates isqat (dropping the rest). If it doesn't, 'ila' indicates imtidad.
3
Apply to oaths and the dispute with Zufar
In an oath 'I will not speak to him up to a month', the Hanafi school holds that speaking on the last day breaks the oath (limit included). Zufar disagreed, holding that 'ila' always indicates termination, so the limit is excluded.
The Hanafi position relies on the principle that when 'ila' conveys isqat, the limit enters.
4
Analyze Quranic and hadith evidence
In wudu verse 'wash your hands to the elbows', the elbows are included because without 'ila' the obligation would cover the entire arm. Similarly, in the hadith 'awra is from navel to knee', the knee is included. Both use 'ila' for isqat.
This reasoning affirms that the context indicates isqat, not imtidad.
5
Understand the third function: ta'khir (postponement)
When 'ila' is used temporally, as in 'you are divorced up to a month', the divorce takes effect after the month, not immediately. This is a separate function from imtidad/isqat.
Ta'khir applies mainly in temporal contexts and delays the ruling until the limit is reached.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل «إلى» لانتهاء الغاية ثم هو في بعض الصور يفيد معنى امتداد الحكم وفي بعض الصوّر يفيد معنى الإسقاط، فإن أفاد الامتداد لا تدخل الغاية في حكم المغيا وإن أفاد الإسقاط تدخل، نظير الأوّل: اشتريت هذا المكان إلى هذا الحائط، لا يدخل الحائط في البيع، ونظير الثاني: باع بشرط الخيار إلى ثلاثة أيام فتدخل الغاية أي اليوم الثالث في الحكم. وبمثله لو حلف لا أكلم فلانا إلى شهر كان الشهر داخلا في الحكم، عندنا خلافا لزفر رحمه الله. وقد أفاد فائدة الإسقاط ههنا وعلى هذا قلنا المرفق والكعب داخلان تحت حكم الغسل في قوله تعالى: إلَى الْمَرَافِق [المائدة: 6]؛ لأنّ كلمة «إلى» ههنا للإسقاط فإنّه لولاها لاستوعبت الوظيفة جميع اليد، ولهذا قلنا: «الركبة من العورة؛ لأن كلمة «إلى» في قوله عليه السلام: «عورة الرجل ما تحت السرة إلى الركبة تفيد فائدة الإسقاط فتدخل الركبة في الحكم، وقد تفيد كلمة «إلى» تأخير الحكم إلى الغاية، ولهذا قلنا: إذا قال لامرأته: «أنت طالق إلى شهر» فلا تكون السرة عورة بل ما تحتها.
Chapter: 'Ila' for the termination of the limit. Then it sometimes conveys the meaning of extension of the ruling, and sometimes conveys the meaning of dropping (the ruling). If it conveys extension, the limit does not enter into the ruling of the limited. If it conveys dropping, the limit enters. Example of the first: I bought this place up to this wall — the wall does not enter into the sale. Example of the second: He sold with a condition of option up to three days — so the limit, i.e., the third day, enters into the ruling. Similarly, if he swore: 'I will not speak to so-and-so up to a month' — the month enters into the ruling, according to us, contrary to Zufar, may Allah have mercy on him. And it has conveyed the benefit of dropping here. And on this basis we say: the elbows and ankles are included under the ruling of washing in the saying of Allah, the Most High: 'to the elbows' [al-Ma'ida 5:6], because the word 'ila' here is for dropping; for were it not for it, the function would encompass the entire hand. And for this we say: 'the knee is part of the 'awra,' because the word 'ila' in his saying, peace be upon him: 'The 'awra of a man is what is between the navel and the knee,' conveys the benefit of dropping, so the knee enters into the ruling. And the word 'ila' may convey the postponement of the ruling to the limit. For this we say: if he says to his wife: 'You are divorced up to a month,' then the navel is not 'awra, but what is below it.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi discusses the Arabic particle 'ila' (to/up to) when used to specify a limit (ghaya). The author explains that 'ila' has two distinct legal functions depending on context. First, it may indicate the extension (imtidad) of a ruling up to a limit, in which case the limit itself is not included in the ruling. For example, if someone buys property 'up to a wall,' the wall is not part of the sale. Second, it may indicate the dropping (isqat) of a ruling beyond the limit, in which case the limit is included in the ruling. For example, if someone sells with an option 'up to three days,' the third day is included. This function is also seen in oaths: swearing not to speak 'up to a month' includes the month. The Hanafi position (contra Zufar) is that the limit enters in such cases. The same principle is applied to the Quranic verse on wudu: 'wash your hands to the elbows' means the elbows are included, because the word 'ila' here drops the obligation from beyond the elbows. Similarly, in the hadith on 'awra (private parts): 'from the navel to the knee,' the knee is included. Finally, 'ila' can also indicate postponement (ta'khir) of a ruling, as in divorce 'up to a month' — the divorce takes effect after the month, not immediately. Thus, the meaning of 'ila' must be determined by context.

Key Terms
إلى (ila): A preposition indicating the limit (ghaya) of a thing; used in legal contexts to define the endpoint of a ruling.
الغاية (al-ghaya): The limit or endpoint mentioned after 'ila'.
امتداد (imtidad): Extension of the ruling up to the limit, where the limit is not included.
إسقاط (isqat): Dropping of the ruling beyond the limit, where the limit itself is included.
تأخير (ta'khir): Postponement of the ruling until the limit is reached.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, when the particle 'ila' is used to indicate isqat (dropping of ruling beyond the limit), does the limit itself fall under the ruling?
A) No, the limit is never included.
B) Yes, but only if the limit is of the same genus as the preceding.
C) Yes, the limit is included because 'ila' is for termination.
D) Yes, the limit is included because the purpose is to drop the ruling beyond it.

Key Takeaway
The particle 'ila' requires context to determine whether the limit is included or excluded, and the Hanafi school applies the principle of isqat when the initial statement covers beyond the limit.

The Preposition 'Ala as a Conditional Particle in Hanafi Usul
Textual analysis of the preposition 'ala with Quranic evidence and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the Arabic preposition 'ala functions as a conditional particle and apply the Hanafi linguistic principle to derive a ruling.

Core Idea
'Ala can mean condition, not exchange—linguistic context determines the legal effect.

In this passage from Usul al-Shashi, the author examines the preposition 'ala. Is it a conditional particle or a substitute for 'with'? The answer determines whether a wife's offer of money for a triple divorce binds her financially. Imam Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn differ, each relying on a distinct linguistic analysis. By tracing the argument, you will see how usul al-fiqh governs fiqh rulings through precise interpretation of language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic issue
The passage asks: what does 'ala mean in the phrase 'Divorce me three times for one thousand'? Is it a condition or an exchange indicator?
2
Examine the Quranic precedent
Quran 60:12 uses 'ala to indicate a condition. Note the structure: allegiance is pledged 'ala that they will not associate partners. This shows 'ala can mean condition.
The verse is a dalil for the conditional usage.
3
State Abu Hanifa's position
He holds that no money is due after one divorce because 'ala implies a condition (shart), and the condition (three divorces) is unmet.
4
Contrast the Sahibayn's view
They interpret 'ala as 'with' (ba), making it an exchange; each divorce triggers a portion of the money.
5
Analyze the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa argues that exchange requires simultaneity, but here divorce and money are sequential. Therefore, 'ala must be a condition. Conclude with the practical consequence (thamarah).
This usul principle—sequential vs. simultaneous—is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل كلمة على للإلزام يكون «على» بمعنى الشرط قال الله تعالى: ﴿ يُبَايِعْنَكَ عَلَى أَن لَّا يُشْرِكْنَ بِالله شَيْئاً﴾ [الممتحنة: ۱۲] ولهذا قال أبو حنيفة: إذا قالت لزوجها: «طلقني ثلاثاً على ألف فطلقها واحدة لا يجب المال؛ لأنّ الكلمة ههنا تفيد معنى الشرط فيكون الثلاث شرطا للزوم المال. فإذا طلقها واحدة لم يوجد الشرط فلا يلزم المال. قوله: [لا يجب المال] وهو ألف للزوج، وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله يجب ثلث الألف. قوله: [فيكون الثلث شرطاً ... إلخ فإنه إذا طلقها الرجل واحدةً لا يجب شيء من المال؛ لأنّ الشرط إذا فات وهو الثلث فات المشروط أي: المال، وعندهما يجب ثلث الألف كما لو قالت: «طلّقني ثلاثـــاً بالألف»؛ لأنّ الطلاق على المال معاوضة من جانب المرأة، والمال يجب عليها عوضاً عن الطلاق، فيحمل كلمة «على» بمعنى الباء، وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله : ليس بين الطلاق وبين المال مقابلة لينعقـــــد معاوضة بل بينهما معاقبة؛ لأنّ الطلاق يجب أوّلاً ثُمّ يجب المال؛ لأنّ المال يجب أوّلاً ثُمّ يقع الطلاق، وهذا معنى الشرط والجزاء لا معنى المعاوضة؛ لأنّ العوض يجب مقابلاً للمعوّض معاً بلا ترتيب تحقيقاً للمقابلة ويحمل على الشرط، فيكون الثلاث شرطاً للزوم المال، فإذا طلقها واحدةً لم يوجد الشرط فلم يلزم المال، كذا في "المعدن".
Chapter: The word 'ala for obligation. 'Ala is used in the sense of condition. Allah Almighty said: {They pledge to you that they will not associate anything with Allah} (60:12). For this reason Abu Hanifa said: If a woman says to her husband, 'Divorce me three times for one thousand,' and he divorces her once, the money is not obligatory because the word here conveys the meaning of condition, so the three is a condition for the obligation of the money. So if he divorces her once, the condition is not met, so the money is not obligatory. [Comment: 'The money is not obligatory' i.e., the one thousand for the husband. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) said: one-third of the thousand is obligatory. [Comment: 'So the three is a condition...' etc. So if the man divorces her once, nothing of the money is obligatory because when the condition (the three) is absent, the conditioned (the money) is absent. According to them, one-third is obligatory, as if she said, 'Divorce me three times for one thousand' because divorce for money is an exchange from the woman's side, and the money is obligatory upon her in exchange for divorce, so the word 'ala is interpreted as 'with'. Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) said: There is no direct correspondence between divorce and money to constitute an exchange; rather there is a sequence. Divorce occurs first, then the money becomes obligatory; or the money is obligatory first, then divorce occurs. This is the meaning of condition and result, not the meaning of exchange. In exchange, the compensation is given in return for the exchanged item simultaneously without sequence to realize the exchange. So it is interpreted as condition, thus the three is a condition for the obligation of the money. If he divorces her once, the condition is not met, so the money is not obligatory. As stated in al-Ma'din.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the Arabic preposition 'ala (على) used in a specific legal context. The author explains that 'ala can function as a conditional particle (بمعنى الشرط), citing Quran 60:12 as evidence. The ruling then applies this to a hypothetical divorce scenario: a wife says 'Divorce me three times for one thousand dirhams' (فيقول: طلقني ثلاثاً على ألف), and the husband divorces her only once. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, no money is owed because the condition (three divorces) was not fulfilled. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) argue that one-third of the money is owed, interpreting 'ala as 'with' (باء), implying an exchange where the money is compensation for each divorce. Abu Hanifa counters that the relationship is one of condition and result (شرط وجزاء), not exchange (معاوضة), because the two obligations are sequential, not simultaneous. Therefore, the money is only due if the condition (three divorces) is met. This analysis demonstrates how linguistic interpretation (usul) directly impacts fiqh rulings. The passage concludes by referencing the compilation al-Ma'din for further study.

Key Terms
على ('ala): Preposition used here in the sense of a condition (shart), rather than its primary meaning of 'upon' or 'for'.
شرط (shart): A condition that must be fulfilled for the obligation (mal) to arise.
معاوضة (muawadah): Exchange contract where consideration is given simultaneously for the countervalue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a wife says 'Divorce me three times for one thousand' and the husband divorces her once, why is no money owed?
A) Because the husband did not intend to pay.
B) Because the word 'ala here means condition; the condition of three divorces was not fulfilled.
C) Because the one thousand is too much for a single divorce.
D) Because the wife did not explicitly say 'with one thousand'.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the word 'ala can function as a conditional particle; its interpretation depends on context and the sequential vs. simultaneous nature of obligations.

The Legal Effect of the Preposition 'Fi' in Temporal Expressions
Explaining that 'fi indicates container and its application in possession and divorce timing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Abu Hanifa's usul principle regarding the preposition 'fi' to determine the temporal scope of legal actions such as divorce and vows, and distinguish it from the Sahibayn's view.

Core Idea
The omission of 'fi' turns a time adverb into a direct object requiring full encompassment; its inclusion keeps it a container allowing any part.

The simple preposition 'fi' (في) carries significant legal weight in the Hanafi tradition. In this lesson, we examine how its presence or absence in temporal expressions—such as divorce statements—alters the judicial interpretation. Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (the Sahibayn) differed on this point, and their difference has practical consequences in court. We will trace the reasoning and apply it to analogous cases like vows of fasting.

Guided Path
1
Recognize 'fi' as a container (zarf)
Understand that the primary meaning of 'fi' is to indicate container, encompassing time, place, or action. This sets the stage for its legal application.
The concept of zarf is central to the analysis.
2
Apply to property case: theft of container
Note the example: 'I stole a cloth in a handkerchief' – both items are owed. This shows that 'fi' includes the container.
3
Identify the dispute in temporal statements
Contrast the two views: the Sahibayn consider deletion or insertion of 'fi' irrelevant; Abu Hanifa holds that deletion (without 'fi') turns the time adverb into a direct object requiring full encompassment.
The difference hinges on grammatical case.
4
Analyze the reasoning: direct object analogy
Learn Abu Hanifa's analogy: a direct object (maf'ul bih) requires the action to apply to the whole object; similarly, a time adverb without 'fi' demands the action cover the entire period.
This is an application of qiyas in usul.
5
Apply the principle to analogous cases
See the fasting analogy: 'I will fast the entire time' vs. 'in the entire time'. The first implies continuous fasting, the second allows any moment.
Fakhr al-Islam uses this to support Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل كلمة في للظرف أي كون في للظرفية. وباعتبار هذا الأصل قال أصحابنا: إذا قال «غصبت ثوبا في منديل أو تمرا في قوصرة» لزماه جميعا، ثم هذه الكلمة دعاء التمر أي لزوم ردهما إلى المالك. كلمة في تستعمل في الزمان والمكان والفعل، أمّا إذا استعملت في الزمان بأن يقول: «أنت طالق غدا»، فقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : يستوي في ذلك حذفها وإظهارها أي في. حتى لو قال: «أنت طالق في غد» كان بمنزلة قوله: «أنت طالق غدا» يقـع على استواء جهتي الحذف والإظهار . أما إذا استعملت في الزمان بأن يقول: «أنت طالق غدا»، فقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : يستوي في ذلك حذفها وإظهارها أي في. حتى لو قال: «أنت طالق في غد» كان بمنزلة قوله: «أنت طالق غدا» يقع على استواء جهتي الحذف والإظهار . أما إذا استعملت في الزمان بأن يقول: «أنت طالق غدا»، فقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : يستوي في ذلك حذفها وإظهارها أي في. حتى لو قال: «أنت طالق في غد» كان بمنزلة قوله: «أنت طالق غدا» يقع على استواء جهتي الحذف والإظهار . وأما عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله إذا حذفت «في» واتصل الفعل بالظرف بأن قال: «أنـــــت طالق غداً فيراد به الاستيعاب إن أمكن؛ لأنّه حينئذ شابه المفعول به حيث انتصب بالفعل فيقتضي الاستيعاب كالمفعول به يقتضي تعلّق الفعل بمجموعه إن أمكن، فإذا قال: «نويت آخـــــر النــــهار» لا يصدق قضاء؛ لأنه غير موجب كلامه، فلابد أن يقع الطلاق في أوّل النهار، وأما إذا اتصل الفعل بـــه بواسطة «في» اقتضى وقوعه في جزء من النهار؛ إذ ليس من ضرورة الظرفية الاستيعاب، فإذا قال: «في غد» وقال: «أردت آخر «النهار» يصدق قضاءً كما يصدّق ديانةً لوقوعه في جزء مبهم من الغد ولــه ولاية التعيين، ونظير هذا لأصومن الدهر» و «في الدهر»، فإنّ الأوّل يقتضي استيعاب العمر بخلاف الثاني، فإنّه يقع على الساعة، كذا قال فخر الإسلام رحمه الله.
Chapter: The word 'fi for container. That is, 'fi is for container (zarfiyya). Based on this principle, our scholars (ashab) said: If someone says, 'I stole a cloth in a handkerchief' or 'dates in a container,' both are obliged (to be returned). Then this word [indicates] the container of the dates, i.e., the obligation to return both to the owner. The word 'fi is used for time, place, and action. When used for time, such as saying 'You are divorced tomorrow,' Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: Deleting it and showing it are equal. So if he says 'You are divorced in tomorrow,' it is equivalent to his saying 'You are divorced tomorrow' and it occurs equally in both cases of deletion and showing. As for Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him): If 'fi is deleted and the verb is directly connected to the time adverb, such as saying 'You are divorced tomorrow' (without fi), it indicates encompassment if possible, because it then resembles the direct object, which requires the verb to apply to the whole if possible. So if he says 'I intended the end of the day,' he is not believed judicially because it does not fulfill his statement; thus the divorce must occur at the beginning of the day. But if the verb is connected to it by means of 'fi, it requires occurrence in a part of the day, since encompassment is not necessary for the container. So if he says 'in tomorrow' and says 'I intended the end of the day,' he is believed judicially just as he is believed religiously, because it occurs in an undefined part of tomorrow and he has authority to specify. An analogy is 'I will fast for the entire time' and 'in the entire time': the first requires encompassing the whole lifespan, unlike the second, which applies to any moment. Thus said Fakhr al-Islam (may Allah have mercy on him).
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the preposition 'fi (في), explaining that its primary function is to indicate container (ظرف), encompassing time, place, or action. The author first gives an example from property law: if someone says 'I stole a cloth in a handkerchief' or 'dates in a container,' both items are included in the theft, meaning both must be returned. Then the discussion focuses on the use of 'fi with time in the context of divorce. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that whether 'fi is mentioned or omitted makes no difference; both 'You are divorced tomorrow' and 'You are divorced in tomorrow' have the same effect: the divorce encompasses the entire day. But Imam Abu Hanifa distinguishes: when 'fi is omitted, the time adverb is accusative and functions like a direct object, implying full encompassment (istīāb) of the period; thus divorce occurs at the beginning of the day, and the husband's claim of intending the end is not accepted in court. When 'fi is present, the time is a container and does not require full encompassment; divorce occurs in some part of the day, and the husband's specification of the end is accepted both religiously and judicially. Fakhr al-Islam provides an analogy with fasting: 'I will fast the entire time' implies continuous fasting, while 'I will fast in the entire time' means fasting at any moment within that time. This analysis shows how subtle grammatical differences affect legal rulings.

Key Terms
في (fi): Preposition indicating container (zarf) - used for time, place, or action. When appended to a time adverb, it indicates that the action occurs within that time without necessarily covering the entire period.
ظرف (zarf): Container - a noun that indicates the time or place of an action. In grammar, it is called maf'ul fihi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a husband says 'You are divorced in tomorrow' (with fi) and then claims he intended the end of the day, is he believed in court?
A) Yes, because the presence of 'fi allows the action to occur in any part of the day.
B) No, because the divorce must occur at the beginning of the day.
C) Yes, but only if his wife agrees.
D) No, because 'fi requires the entire day to be included.

Key Takeaway
The presence or absence of 'fi' in temporal expressions changes the legal requirement from full encompassment to partial occurrence, affecting judicial acceptance of intention.

The Legal Effect of 'Fi' in Temporal and Spatial Expressions: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Analysis of the word 'fi' and its effect on the scope of actions in time and place

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle regarding the omission or inclusion of 'في' to determine the scope of an action in time or place, and evaluate the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Omitting 'في' makes a time phrase act like a direct object demanding totality; including it keeps it as a container allowing ambiguity.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi explores a subtle grammatical point with profound legal consequences: does saying 'You are divorced tomorrow' (أنت طالق غدا) differ from 'You are divorced in tomorrow' (أنت طالق في غد)? Abu Hanifa argues that omitting 'في' binds the action to the entire period, while including it permits specification. The Sahibayn see no difference. Understanding this principle is essential for interpreting oaths, divorce pronouncements, and conditional contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic structure
Determine whether the verb is directly attached to the time/place word (omission of 'في') or connected via 'في'. For example, 'أنت طالق غدا' omits 'في', while 'أنت طالق في غد' includes it.
The presence/absence of 'في' is the key variable.
2
Apply the Hanafi rule on omission
If 'في' is omitted, the time/place word resembles a direct object (maf'ul bih) and requires the action to encompass the whole time/place, if possible. Under Abu Hanifa, divorce occurs at the beginning (dawn).
The rule is based on the grammatical shift from zarf to maf'ul bih.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule on inclusion
If 'في' is present, the word remains a pure zarf (container) and does not imply totality. The action occurs in an ambiguous part; the speaker's intention is accepted in court.
Container-ness (zarfiyya) does not require isti'ab (encompassing).
4
Compare with the Sahibayn's view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that omission and inclusion are equal: both imply the entire time, and intention can specify any part. The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, without 'في', the court does not accept a contrary intention.
The thamarah al-khilaf lies in the default ruling and evidentiary weight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل كلمة في للظرف
أي كون في للظرفية. وباعتبار هذا الأصل قال أصحابنا: إذا قال «غصبت ثوبا في منديل أو تمرا في قوصرة» لزماه جميعا، ثم هذه الكلمة تستعمل في الزمان والمكان والفعل، أمّا إذا استعملت في الزمان بأن يقول: «أنت طالق غدا»، فقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : يستوي في ذلك حذفها وإظهارها حتى لو قال: «أنت طالق في غد» كان بمنزلة قوله: «أنت طالق غدا» يقع على استواء جهتي الحذف والإظهار. وأما عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله إذا حذفت «في» واتصل الفعل بالظرف بأن قال: «أنت طالق غدا» فيراد به الاستيعاب إن أمكن؛ لأنه حينئذ شابه المفعول به حيث انتصب بالفعل فيقتضي الاستيعاب كالمفعول به يقتضي تعلّق الفعل بمجموعه إن أمكن، فإذا قال: «نويت آخر النهار» لا يصدق قضاء؛ لأنه غير موجب كلامه، فلابد أن يقع الطلاق في أوّل النهار، وأما إذا اتصل الفعل به بواسطة «في» اقتضى وقوعه في جزء من النهار؛ إذ ليس من ضرورة الظرفية الاستيعاب، فإذا قال: «في غد» وقال: «أردت آخر النهار» يصدق قضاءً كما يصدّق ديانةً لوقوعه في جزء مبهم من الغد وله ولاية التعيين. ونظير هذا «لأصومن الدهر» و «في الدهر»، فإنّ الأوّل يقتضي استيعاب العمر بخلاف الثاني، فإنّه يقع على الساعة.
وذهب أبو حنيفة رحمه الله إلى أنها إذا حذفت يقع الطلاق كما طلع الفجر وإذا أظهرت كان المراد وقوع الطلاق في جزء من الغد على سبيل الإبهام فلو لا وجود النية يقع الطلاق بأوّل الجزء لعدم المزاحم له، ولو نوى آخر النهار صحت نيته. ومثال ذلك في قول الرجل: «إن صمت الشهر فأنت كذا» فإنّه يقع على صوم الشهر بتمامه. ولو قال: «إن صمت في الشهر فأنت كذا» يقع ذلك على الإمساك ساعة. وأمّا في المكان فمثل قوله: «أنت طالق في الدار» أو «في مكة» جزء مبهم من المكان.
Section: The word 'fi' for container. That is, 'fi' denotes container-ness. By virtue of this principle our companions said: If one says 'I usurped a cloth in a handkerchief or dates in a basket' both are incumbent. Then this word is used for time, place, and action. As for when it is used for time by saying: 'You are divorced tomorrow', Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: its omission and mention are equal, such that if he says 'You are divorced in tomorrow' it is in the position of his saying 'You are divorced tomorrow'—it occurs equally between omission and mention. And as for Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, if 'fi' is omitted and the verb is directly connected to the adverb by saying 'You are divorced tomorrow', then it intends encompassing if possible; because then it resembles the direct object as it is in accusative due to the verb, thus it requires encompassing like the direct object requires the verb to attach to the whole if possible. So if he says 'I intended the end of the day' he is not believed in court because it does not necessitate his speech; thus divorce must occur at the beginning of the day. And if the verb is connected to it via 'fi', it requires its occurrence in a part of the day, since it is not necessary for container-ness to encompass. So if he says 'in tomorrow' and says 'I intended the end of the day', he is believed in court just as he is believed religiously because it occurs in an ambiguous part of tomorrow and he has authority to specify. An analogy is 'I will fast the age' and 'in the age'—the first requires encompassing the lifetime, contrary to the second which occurs at an hour. Abu Hanifa held that if 'fi' is omitted, divorce occurs as the dawn breaks; if it is mentioned, the intended meaning is occurrence of divorce in a part of tomorrow ambiguously, so if there is no intent, divorce occurs at the first part due to no competitor, and if he intends the end of the day his intent is valid. An example of this is a man's saying 'If you fast the month, then you are such'—it occurs upon fasting the entire month. And if he said 'If you fast in the month, then you are such' it occurs upon abstaining for an hour. As for place, it is like his saying 'You are divorced in the house' or 'in Mecca'—an ambiguous part of the place.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a usul al-fiqh analysis of the word 'في' (fi) when used for container/zarf, focusing on how its presence or absence affects the legal scope of actions. The text first establishes that 'في' denotes container-ness (ظرفية). Based on this, the Hanafi scholars ruled that if someone says 'I usurped a cloth in a handkerchief' both the cloth and handkerchief are considered usurped together because the container and its contents are both taken. The key legal dispute concerns temporal expressions: when a husband says 'You are divorced tomorrow' (أنت طالق غدا) without 'في', Abu Hanifa holds that the omission of 'في' makes the time phrase resemble a direct object, implying that the divorce occurs at the very beginning of 'tomorrow' (i.e., at dawn), unless the husband states a different intention. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) disagree, asserting that there is no difference whether 'في' is omitted or mentioned; in both cases the divorce is considered to be effective over the entire day of tomorrow, but the husband's specific intention is accepted in court if he says he meant a specific part. The passage provides analogies: fasting for 'the age' (الدهر) vs. 'in the age' (في الدهر) and fasting 'the month' vs. 'in the month'. In all cases, without 'في' the action is understood to encompass the whole time; with 'في' it applies to any part. The text then applies the same principle to place: saying 'divorced in the house' or 'in Mecca' means the divorce occurs in an unspecified part of that place. The commentary from أحسن الحواشی explains that when 'في' is omitted, the word becomes like a direct object requiring full coverage; when present, it is a pure zarf and does not imply coverage. The passage also addresses potential objections, such as the Isra' verse where 'أسرى بعبده' omits 'في' yet did not cover the whole night, responding that the general rule is with 'في' unless evidence indicates otherwise.

Key Terms
ظرف (zarf): A container or adverb; a word that indicates the time or place of an action. Here it is used as a grammatical term for what 'في' introduces.
استيعاب (isti'āb): Encompassing; the action covers the entire time or place introduced by the adverb.
مفعول به (maf'ul bih): Direct object; when a time/place word is treated as a direct object (due to omission of 'في'), it requires the verb to apply to the whole.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a man says 'أنت طالق في غد' (You are divorced in tomorrow) and does not specify a time, when does the divorce occur?
A) At the beginning of tomorrow (dawn).
B) At the end of tomorrow.
C) In an ambiguous part of tomorrow, and if no intention is stated, it occurs at the beginning.
D) The divorce is invalid because 'في' makes it ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The presence or absence of 'في' in temporal and spatial expressions changes the default scope of a legal action in Hanafi usul: omission implies totality, inclusion implies ambiguity.

The Rule of Omission and Inclusion of 'في' in Oaths
Explaining the Hanafi rule on how the preposition 'في' affects the scope of actions in time and place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule distinguishing between omission and inclusion of the preposition 'في' to determine the scope of an action in time or place in oaths and vows.

Core Idea
Omitting 'في' implies full scope; including it limits to a part — a key linguistic principle in Hanafi usul.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the presence or absence of the preposition 'في' (in) when an action is linked to a time or place determines whether the action covers the entire period/location or only a part. This lesson explores that rule, its linguistic basis, its application in oaths and divorces, and the response to a Qur'anic objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify presence/absence of 'في'
Examine the wording of the oath or vow to see whether the preposition 'في' is included or omitted before the time or place.
In Arabic, 'في' is the particle for 'in' indicating adverbial relationship.
2
Apply the default rule
If 'في' is omitted, the action is taken to encompass the entire time or place (isti'ab). If included, the action covers only a part (juz').
The rule is based on linguistic analysis: without 'في', the word resembles a direct object requiring full scope; with 'في', it is purely adverbial.
3
Consider exceptions
The default rule may be overridden by specific evidence (dalil) indicating otherwise, as in the Night Journey verse where omission did not imply comprehensiveness because ahadith specified it was only part of the night.
The Hanafi school acknowledges that the rule is not absolute; it holds unless proven otherwise.
4
Apply to time and place
For time, the rule applies straightforwardly. For place, note that divorce added to a place results in immediate divorce in all places because location does not specify divorce; however, if a conditional intention is present, it can alter the ruling.
The passage distinguishes between time as a suitable adverb and place as unsuitable for divorce.
5
Extend to transitive/intransitive verbs
For intransitive actions, the condition is the agent's presence in the time/place; for transitive actions, the condition is the object's presence, since the action is identified by its effect on the object.
This part of the passage expands the rule from static nouns to verbs in oaths.

(٣) قوله: [يتعدى... إلخ] أي: يتوقف وجوده إلى مفعول كالضرب والشجّ والقتل مثلاً، فإنهما لا تـتـمّ بوجود الفاعل وحده، بل يتوقف على وجود المفعول وهو المضروب والمشجوج والمقتول. ١٢ (٤) قوله : الأنّ الفعل إنّما يتحقق... إلخ لأنّ الأفعال إنما تعرف بظهر آثارها في محلّها ألا ترى أنه تختلف أسمائها باختلاف آثارها، فإنّ من أرسل خشبة من الأعلى على غيره فإنّ أثره في الإيلام يسمّى ضرباً وإن أثره في الجرح يسمّى جرحاً وإنّ أثره في إزهاق الروح يسمّى قتلاً، ولما اختلف الفعل باختلاف آثارها علمنا أنّ اسم الفعل باعتبار ما ثبت بالمفعول به فيراعي المكان في حقه، ونحن نقول بهذا الاستدلال خرج الجواب عما قيل: إنّ هذه الأفعال تتمّ بالفاعل والمفعول لا بأحدهما، فيجب أن يراعي المكان في حقهما؛ لأنّ اسم الفعل لم يثبت إلا لمعنى اختص بالمفعول وهو أثر الفعل، هذا توضيح ما في
"المعدن" . ١٢
Chapter: The word 'في' for the adverb of time/place. Divorce is like the dawn in both forms, and Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) held that the omission and inclusion [of 'في'] are from the wording, not intention. [Footnote: i.e., in. From the wording, not intention. By its mere rising. i.e., the intended meaning. That when it is omitted, divorce occurs as the dawn rises, and when included, the intended meaning is the occurrence of divorce in a part of tomorrow on the basis of ambiguity; if there were no intention, divorce would occur because it exists and other is non-existent, so no competition. Because it specifies one of its probabilities. At the first part due to no competitor, and if he intended the end of the day, his intention is valid, and example of that is the difference between omission and inclusion. i.e., 'you are divorced' for example. According to Abu Hanifa. In a man's saying: 'If you fast the month, then you get such-and-such', it occurs on fasting the whole month. i.e., fasting a day from it. And if he said: 'If you fast in the month, then you get such-and-such', it occurs on refraining for an hour. Its usage. 'You are divorced' in the month. As for place, similar to his saying: 'You are divorced in the house' and 'in Makkah' an ambiguous part of... (Footnote 1: [His saying: 'in a part of tomorrow on the basis of ambiguity' etc.] Because if it is mentioned, divorce becomes added to tomorrow, and that does not require comprehensiveness. If it is said: 'Tomorrow is an adverb in both estimations, what is the secret that omitting 'في' necessitates comprehensiveness and including it does not?', it is said: 'When 'في' is omitted from it, it resembles the object; when 'في' is mentioned, what it enters upon becomes purely for adverbiality, and the adverb does not necessitate comprehensiveness.' This is in most commentaries and in Al-Ma'dan. If it is said: 'This is problematic in the noble verse: 'Glory be to Him who took His servant by night' [al-Isra:1] where 'في' is omitted and the night journey did not encompass all the night but was in part of it as known in the story of the Mi'raj', it is said: 'The principle is that omitting 'في' necessitates comprehensiveness according to Abu Hanifa's principle unless there is evidence, and the reports are widespread that the night journey was only in part of the night.' End.) (Footnote 2: [His saying: 'His intention is valid'] Because he specified one of its probabilities without changing its implicative meaning, and because when the word 'في' appears, it becomes purely for adverbiality, and the adverb of divorce is only in a part of tomorrow, and that does not require comprehensiveness.) (Footnote 3: [His saying: 'occurs on fasting' etc.] i.e., the condition for his breaking the oath is fasting the entire month without an intervening particle, so it necessitates comprehensiveness of the month by fasting.) (Footnote 4: [His saying: 'occurs on refraining' etc.] Because the action is added to an ambiguous part of the month, and that does not require comprehensiveness.) [Page 182] Principles of Al-Shashi with Ahsan Al-Hawashi. i.e., the added divorce. Clarification of absoluteness. Plural of places. Chapter: The word 'في' for the adverb. In the word 'في'. That becomes an absolute divorce in all places, and by virtue of the meaning of adverbiality from the restraint of marriage. Because the place is not suitable as an adverb for it. i.e., the action. We said: If he swears an oath on an action and adds it to a time or place, then if the action is something that is complete (i.e., does not need an object) by the adverbial addition, it is required that the agent be in that time or place; and if the action is transitive to an object (i.e., its effect exceeds), it is required that the object be in that time or place; because the action is only realized by its effect in its locus. (Footnote 1: [His saying: 'That becomes' etc.] i.e., divorce when added to a place occurs immediately because the place is not suitable as an adverb for divorce; for the adverb to a thing is like the description to it, and whatever is a description of something must be suitable for specification, and the place is not suitable as a specification for divorce at all, because if it occurs in one place, it occurs in all places, as no place is preferable over another with respect to effecting divorce, but all are equal in this, unlike time... etc.) (Footnote 2: [His saying: 'in all places' etc.] A substitute for his saying: 'on absolute terms', i.e., divorce occurs in all places immediately, because the place is not suitable as an adverb for divorce; for the adverb to a thing is like the description to it, and whatever is a description of something must be suitable for specification, and the described is specified by the description and attributed to it, and the place is not suitable for divorce at all; because if it occurs in a place, divorce occurs in it when added to the place, so if he says: 'You are divorced in the house', it occurs immediately unless he intends to imply the action, meaning 'upon your entering the house', then it becomes conditional, so she is not divorced immediately; because he mentioned the locus and intended the present action, he is believed between him and Allah because the wording can bear it, but it is against the apparent meaning, so it is not accepted judicially.) (Footnote 3: [His saying: 'transitive' etc.] i.e., its existence depends on an object, like hitting, wounding, killing for example, for they are not completed by the agent alone, but depend on the existence of the object, which is the hit, wounded, killed.) (Footnote 4: [His saying: 'the action is only realized' etc.] Because actions are known by the appearance of their effects in their loci; do you not see that their names differ according to their effects? For one who sends a piece of wood from above onto another, if its effect is causing pain, it is called hitting; if its effect is wounding, it is called wounding; if its effect is killing, it is called killing. Since the action differs according to its effects, we know that the name of the action is based on what is established in the object, so place is considered with respect to it. And we say: by this inference, the answer emerges to what was said: 'These actions are completed by the agent and object, not by one of them, so the place must be considered with respect to both.' Because the name of the action is only established for a meaning specific to the object, which is the effect of the action. This is the clarification of what is in Al-Ma'dan.)
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage from al-Shashi's Usul al-Fiqh, with the commentary 'Ahsan al-Hawashi', explains a key linguistic principle in the Hanafi school regarding the preposition 'في' (meaning 'in') when used as a zarf (adverb of time or place). The central rule is that the presence or omission of 'في' affects whether an action is considered to encompass the entire time/place or only a part. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that when 'في' is omitted, the action is taken to cover the full duration or entire location, whereas when it is included, it indicates only a part. This is derived from the fact that without 'في', the time/place resembles a direct object (maf'ul bih), which implies full scope, while with 'في', it is purely adverbial and does not imply comprehensiveness. The text illustrates this with examples from divorce and oaths: if a man says 'You are divorced tomorrow' (omitting 'في'), the divorce occurs at the first part of the day; if he says 'You are divorced in tomorrow' (including 'في'), it occurs in an unspecified part. Similarly, for fasting: 'If you fast the month' means the whole month, while 'If you fast in the month' means any part. The same logic applies to place, but with a distinction: since place is not a suitable adverb for the act of divorce (divorce is not localized), adding 'في' to a place results in immediate divorce in all places, unless the speaker intends a conditional meaning (e.g., 'in your entering the house'). The commentary also addresses an objection from the Qur'anic verse about the Night Journey (al-Isra 1), where 'في' is omitted yet the journey did not cover all night; the response is that Abu Hanifa's principle stands unless there is evidence to the contrary, and here the evidence (ahadith) specifies it was only part of the night. The later part of the passage expands the rule to verbs: if an oath involves an intransitive action (complete without object), the agent's presence in the time/place is required; if transitive, the object's presence is required, since the action is identified by its effect on the object.

Key Terms
في (fī): A preposition used to indicate adverbial relationship (zarf) for time or place.
حذف (hadhf): Omission of the preposition 'في' in an oath or vow.
إظهار (izhār): Inclusion or appearance of the preposition 'في' in an oath or vow.
ظرف (zarf): An adverb of time or place; a container noun indicating when or where an action occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A man says 'إن صمت الشهر فأنت كذا' (If you fast the month, you get such-and-such). According to Abu Hanifa, what must he do to fulfill the oath?
A) Fast the entire month
B) Fast any day of the month
C) Fast the whole month consecutively
D) Fast at least half the month

Key Takeaway
The presence or absence of 'في' in oaths determines whether the action must cover the entire time/place or only a part, with exceptions overridden by specific evidence.

The Word 'Fi' in Oaths: Container Attachment Rule
Explaining the legal effect of 'fi' in conditional oaths involving place and time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for interpreting 'fi' in conditional oaths to determine whether the oath is broken, by distinguishing intransitive and transitive verbs and identifying the container's attachment.

Core Idea
In oaths with 'fi', the container attaches to the subject for intransitive verbs and to the object for transitive verbs.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi explains a key Hanafi usul rule governing conditional oaths that specify a time or place using the word 'fi'. The rule, transmitted from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, depends on whether the verb is intransitive (lazim) or transitive (muta'addi). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying oath-related rulings in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the verb type
Determine whether the verb in the oath is intransitive (lazim) or transitive (muta'addi). Intransitive verbs are complete with only the subject; transitive verbs require a direct object.
Example: 'abuse' (شتم) is intransitive; 'strike' (ضرب) is transitive.
2
Determine the essential element
For intransitive verbs, the essential element is the subject (doer). For transitive verbs, it is the object (the one affected).
The effect of the action resides in that element.
3
Attach the container
The place or time specified after 'fi' attaches to that essential element.
Thus 'in the mosque' qualifies the subject for intransitive verbs and the object for transitive verbs.
4
Verify the condition
For the oath to be broken, that essential element must be present in the specified container at the time of the action.
If the essential element is not in the container, the oath is not broken.
5
Test with the text's examples
Apply the rule to each example: abusing (intransitive → subject must be in mosque), striking/wounding (transitive → object must be in mosque), killing (death event must occur on specified day).
All examples from Al-Jami' al-Kabir confirm the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل كلمة في للظرف
قلنا : إذا حلف على فعل وأضافه إلى زمان أو مكان فإن كان الفعل ممّا يتمّ بالفاعل يشترط كون الفاعل في ذلك الزمان أو المكان، وإن كان الفعل يتعدّى إلى محل يشترط كون المحلّ في ذلك الزمان والمكان؛ لأنّ الفعل إنّما يتحقق بأثره وأثره في المحل. قال محمد رحمه الله في "الجامع الكبير": إذا قال: «إن شتمتك في المسجد فكذا» فشتمه وهو في المسجد والمشتوم خارج المسجد يحنث ولو كان الشاتم خارج المسجد والمشتوم في المسجد لا يحنث، ولو قال: «إن ضربتك» أو «شججتك في المسجد فكذا يشترط كون المضروب والمشجوج في المسجد ولا يشترط كون الضارب والشاج فيه، ولو قال: «إن قتلتك في يوم الخميس فكذا فجرحه قبل يوم الخميس ومات يوم الخميس يحنث ولو جرحه يوم الخميس ومات يوم الجمعة لا يحنث.
Chapter: The word 'fi' for container. We say: If one swears upon an action and adds it to a time or place, then if the action is completed by the doer, it is required that the doer be in that time or place. And if the action is transitive to an object, it is required that the object be in that time and place, because the action is only realized by its effect and its effect is in the object. Muhammad [al-Shaybani], may Allah have mercy on him, said in Al-Jami' al-Kabir: If one says: 'If I abuse you in the mosque, then such-and-such' and he abuses him while he [the abuser] is in the mosque and the abused is outside the mosque, he violates the oath. And if the abuser is outside the mosque and the abused is in the mosque, he does not violate. And if he says: 'If I strike you' or 'wound you in the mosque, then such-and-such', it is required that the struck and wounded be in the mosque, and it is not required that the striker and wounder be in it. And if he says: 'If I kill you on Thursday, then such-and-such' and he wounds him before Thursday and he dies on Thursday, he violates. And if he wounds him on Thursday and he dies on Friday, he does not violate.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul rule about the interpretation of the word 'fi' (in) used in conditional oaths involving a place or time. The rule distinguishes between intransitive verbs (like abusing) where the condition for the oath to be violated is that the subject (the doer) is present in the specified place/time, and transitive verbs (like striking or killing) where the condition is that the object is present. The reason is that transitive actions are only fully realized by their effect on the object. Imam Muhammad's examples clarify: For abusing (an intransitive verb), only the abuser must be in the mosque. For striking (transitive), the victim must be in the mosque. For killing on a specific day, the key is death—if death occurs on the specified day, the oath is broken; if death occurs later, it is not. This demonstrates that the container (place/time) attaches to the verb's essential element: for intransitive verbs, the subject; for transitive, the object. The ruling is from Al-Jami' al-Kabir, part of Zahir al-Riwayah, thus authoritative.

Key Terms
في (fi): A preposition meaning 'in', used in oaths to specify a container (place or time) for the action swore upon.
لازم (lazim): An intransitive verb that is completed by the subject alone without needing a direct object.
متعد (muta'addi): A transitive verb that requires a direct object to complete its meaning.
الظرف (al-zarf): The container; here, the time or place indicated by 'fi' that the action is attributed to.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) In the mosque
B) Outside the mosque
C) Anywhere, as long as the striker is in the mosque
D) The oath is never broken because striking is an imaginary action

Key Takeaway
The word 'fi' in oaths attaches to the subject for intransitive verbs and to the object for transitive verbs, determined by where the verb's effect resides.

The Letter Ba for Adhesion: Hanafi Usul and the Wiping Dispute
Defining the ba as for adhesion and refuting Shafi'i's view of partiality in wiping the head

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the primary meaning of the letter ba according to Hanafi usul, apply it to the Quranic verse of wiping the head, and evaluate the Shafi'i and Maliki interpretations.

Core Idea
The ba always means adhesion; the Quran's use confirms wiping must adhere to the head, not merely touch part of it.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi establishes the letter ba as a particle of adhesion (ilṣāq) by linguistic consensus. It then applies this principle to a famous fiqh dispute: the interpretation of 'bi-ru’ūsikum' in the wudu verse (Quran 5:6). The Hanafi school insists on the literal meaning of ba as adhesion, rejecting the Shafi'i claim of partiality and the Maliki claim of it being extra. Understanding this usul rule unlocks the correct derivation of rulings from Quranic commands.

Guided Path
1
Define the ba's primary meaning
Identify from the passage that the letter ba is unanimously considered for adhesion (ilṣāq) by linguists, and that all other meanings (partiality, extra, etc.) are metaphorical.
This is the bedrock: the ba in Arabic is not for partiality or extra unless compelled by context.
2
Locate the Shafi'i and Maliki interpretations
Find in the text where Imam al-Shafi'i claims the ba in 'bi-ru’ūsikum' indicates partiality (tabʿīḍ) and where Imam Malik claims it is extra (ṣilah). Note their resulting rulings: Shafi'i => wiping a part suffices; Malik => whole head must be wiped.
These are the main opposing views that the Hanafi commentator refutes.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
Understand the Hanafi argument: (a) partiality would cause synonymy with the particle 'min' and polysemy, both avoidable; (b) extra is contrary to the original meaning. Therefore, the ba retains its literal meaning of adhesion.
The Hanafi method prioritizes the literal (haqiqah) over the metaphorical (majaz) unless a clear contextual indicator (qarina) exists.
4
Apply the principle to the contract of sale
Examine how the ba in 'bāʻa bi-al-thaman' (sold with the price) also indicates adhesion: the action of selling adheres to the price. The text explains that the sold item is the principal and the price is dependent, as seen in the rule that destruction of the sold item voids the sale but destruction of the price does not.
This shows the consistency of the usul principle across different domains.
5
Synthesize the derivation chain
Combine the steps: (1) Identify the ba in the Quranic verse; (2) Classify it as a particle of adhesion based on consensus; (3) Apply the usul rule that haqiqah is preferred; (4) Conclude that the wiping must adhere to the head; (5) Note that the extent (quarter) is determined by other proofs (sunnah), not by the ba itself.
This chain demonstrates how usul al-fiqh transforms a simple letter into a precise legal guideline.

غيره من نسخ الأصول أنّ ما دخل عليه الباء هو الملصق به والطرف الآخر الملصق، كما ، كما في قولك: كتبت بالقلم» معناه الصقت الكتابة بالقلم، والجواب عنه: أن المقصود إيصال الفعل إلى الاسم دون عكسه، إذ المقصود من قولك: كتبت بالقلم» و«أخــبرت بالقدوم» هو
Section: The letter ba for adhesion, meaning by consensus of the people of language, and the other meanings are metaphorical. i.e., it enters into them. Section: The letter ba is for adhesion in the linguistic usage, and for this reason it accompanies prices, because it is for adhesion. Because it is contracted upon as intended. As if it is outside the contracted upon and dependent upon it. The verification of this: The sold item is the principal in a sale and the price is a condition in it, and for this meaning the destruction of the sold item necessitates the rescission of the sale, not the destruction of the price. When this is established, we say: The principal (i.e., the price) i.e., the sold item (i.e., the sold item) that the dependent be attached to the principal, not the principal be attached to the dependent... where (1) His statement: [for adhesion] i.e., the attachment of one thing to another and its connection to it. So what the ba enters upon is that to which something is attached, and the other party is the attached. This is its original meaning in language, and the other meanings are metaphorical. (2) His statement: [in linguistic usage] He only said 'in linguistic usage' to indicate the refutation of the statement of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, who claimed that the ba in the verse of Allah 'Wipe your heads' (Quran 5:6) is for partiality, and it is known among the Arabs that this entails synonymy and polysemy, and both are not primary in speech. This is because the letter 'min' is placed for partiality, so if the ba were for partiality, synonymy would be necessary; and because if it were for partiality while it is for adhesion, polysemy would be necessary. So the meaning of the verse according to him is: wipe some of your heads, and 'some' is indefinite, whether it be hair or above it, even close to all. So whichever part you wipe, you are performing the commanded act. And Malik, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'It is a connector', i.e., extra, so the meaning is: wipe your heads, and the apparent meaning is the whole, so wiping the entire head becomes obligatory. We say: It is not like that, i.e., the ba is not for partiality nor for being extra, because partiality is metaphorical, so one should not resort to it, and likewise extra is contrary to the principle. So understand, as in al-Ma'din. (3) His statement: 'and the price is a condition in it'. If it is said: Just as a sale does not exist without the sold item, likewise it does not exist without the price, so how can he say it is a condition? We say: The price is dependent and the sold item is principal, because the original purpose in a sale is to benefit from the owned item, and that is achieved by the sold item, not by what is a price, because the price is usually currency, which is not beneficial in itself. For this reason, a sale is valid even if the price is not present, while selling what one does not possess is invalid. So it is clear that the sold item is principal and the price is dependent, as in al-Ma'din. (4) His statement: [not that the principal...] And someone might say: Since the attached is dependent and that to which attached is principal, then the price is principal, because the ba enters upon the price and it is that to which attached. Do you not see that your saying 'I passed by Zaid' means my passing adhered to Zaid? It is mentioned in other copies of the Usul that what the ba enters upon is that to which something is attached, and the other party is the attached, as in your saying 'I wrote with the pen' means I adhered the writing to the pen. And the answer to this is that the purpose is to convey the action to the noun, not vice versa, for the purpose of your saying 'I wrote with the pen' and 'I informed of the arrival' is...
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a classic Hanafi usul definition and dispute. It begins by defining the Arabic letter (حرف) 'ب' (ba) as primarily indicating adhesion (إلصاق) or attachment, according to the consensus of linguists. All other meanings, such as partiality (تبعيض) or being extra (صلة), are considered metaphorical. The text then applies this principle to a famous fiqh dispute regarding the verse of wiping the head in wudu (Quran 5:6). Imam al-Shafi'i interpreted the ba in 'بِرُؤُوسِكُمْ' as indicating partiality, meaning only part of the head needs to be wiped. The Hanafi rebuttal, as given in the commentary, is that the ba is for adhesion, not partiality. They argue that if the ba meant partiality, that would cause synonymy with the letter 'من' (min) which is specifically for partiality, and also polysemy, both of which are avoided in language. Imam Malik's view that the ba is extra (صلة/زائدة) is also rejected because it contradicts the original meaning. The Hanafi position is that the ba retains its original meaning of adhesion, implying that the wiping action must adhere to the head, but the extent (whole or part) is determined by other evidence — here, the Hanafi ruling is that wiping a quarter of the head is sufficient based on other proofs, but the text does not delve into that. The second part of the passage discusses the use of ba in contracts of sale. It explains that in the phrase 'بعت بالثمن', the ba indicates adhesion, and the price (ثمن) is attached to the sold item (مبيع). It clarifies that the sold item is the principal (أصل) and the price is a condition (شرط) or dependent (تبَع). This is evidenced by the fact that if the sold item is destroyed, the sale is rescinded, but if the price is destroyed, the sale remains valid (since the price can be replaced). The commentary answers an objection: if ba enters upon the price, why isn't the price the principal? The answer is that the purpose is to attach the action to the noun (the price), not to make the price the principal; the focus is on the action (selling) adhering to the price, but logically the sold item remains the core subject of the contract. This lesson demonstrates the Hanafi methodology of taking the literal meaning (حقيقة) of particles and resisting metaphorical interpretations (مجاز) unless necessary.

Key Terms
الإلصاق (al-ilṣāq): Adhesion: the attachment of one thing to another; the original meaning of the ba.
التبعيض (al-tabʿīḍ): Partiality: indicating part of something; attributed to Shafi'i for the ba in the verse.
الصلة (al-ṣilah): Connective or extra letter; attributed to Malik for the ba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because it would lead to synonymy with 'min' and polysemy.
B) Because it contradicts the consensus of linguists.

Key Takeaway
The letter ba in Arabic is primarily for adhesion, and Hanafi usul insists on preserving this literal meaning to avoid arbitrary metaphorical interpretation, as demonstrated in the wiping verse and sale contracts.

The Preposition Bā as an Indicator of Price in Sales
Explains that in sales, the item preceded by bā is the price and a follower.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that the item preceded by the preposition bā is the price (thaman) in a sale contract, and derive the ruling for substitution before possession.

Core Idea
In Hanafi sales law, the bā marks the price as a follower; the other item is the sold object.

This passage from 'Usul al-Shashi' presents a key Hanafi usul rule: the preposition ba (ب) in sale contracts indicates that the item it precedes is the price (thaman) and a follower (tabi'), while the other item is the sold object. Using linguistic examples like 'I cut with the knife', the author establishes that bā marks the tool/follower, not the primary object. This rule determines which party is the price and affects legal consequences like substitution before delivery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
Recognize that the passage states a rule: when bā enters upon a substitute in a sale, that substitute is the price (thaman) and a follower attached to the original (sold item).
The rule is based on the function of bā for attachment (ilṣāq).
2
Analyze the linguistic analogy
Examine examples like 'I cut with the knife' (قطعت بالسكين). Here, the cutting action attaches to the knife, making the knife a tool/follower. The action itself is the original. Similarly, in sale, the price is a means (tool) to acquire the sold item.
The key insight: the object of bā is the follower, not the original.
3
Apply to the two sale scenarios
Work through the two examples: (1) 'I sold you this slave for a kurr of wheat' – the slave is the sold item, wheat is price (bā enters on wheat). Permissible to substitute wheat before delivery. (2) 'I sold you a kurr of wheat for this slave' – the wheat becomes sold item, slave becomes price; contract becomes salam (deferred).
The reversal changes the nature of the contract entirely.
4
Address the potential objection
Consider the objection: if bā indicates attachment, then the object of bā might be the original (as in 'I passed by Zaid'). The answer: the intended action is to convey the verb to the noun; the noun is the original, and the bā-marked item is the tool/follower.
The resolution relies on the objective of the utterance, not mere grammatical form.
5
Generalize the principle
The rule applies when both countervalues are non-monetary (goods). Whichever side bā enters upon is the price; the other is the sold item. This is a consistent Hanafi usul principle derived from linguistic usage.
This principle is used to classify contracts and determine rules of substitution and salam.

Source Text

Chapter: The letter ba for attachment and its examples from compensations. The entry of ba means that the object of ba is the price attached as a follower. When the letter ba enters the substitute in the chapter of sale, it indicates that it, i.e., the substitute which is the price, is a follower attached to the original, so it is not the sold item but becomes the price. On this basis we say: if one says, 'I sold you this slave for a kurr of wheat and described it,' the slave is the sold item and the kurr is the price, so substitution before possession is permissible. If he said, 'I sold you a kurr of wheat for this slave,' the slave is the price and the kurr is the sold item, and the contract becomes a salam (advance sale) which is only valid when deferred. And 'I cut with the knife' and 'I struck with the sword' and similar examples attach these actions to these things without the reverse, so the attached (the action) is the original and the object of the attachment is the follower, like a tool for the thing. For this reason ba accompanies prices in sales, because the price is not the objective in sale, like a tool for the thing. Thus in 'al-Fusul'. In some of its glosses: the author's expression is taken as inversion, so its estimation is: we say the principle is that the follower is attached to the original; when the letter ba enters in the chapter of sale, it indicates that it is a follower attached to the original. End. But the statement is not free of inconsistency, so reflect on it. [The commentator's words: 'by following', its verification: that the price is a follower because since the price is not connected to the sustenance of the body nor does it inherently achieve survival, it is a subsidiary means to things by which the soul survives, so it is not an intended form; rather the intended thing from it is a more general meaning existing in the price, so the destruction of the specific price does not void the sale. As for the sold item, the intended thing from it is its utility, which is form and value, so its destruction voids the sale. So in 'al-Miftah'. [Commentary on 'in the substitute'] i.e., the substitute of the sold item which is the price. One might object: since the attached is the follower and the object of attachment is the original, the price would be the original, because ba enters upon the price which is the object of attachment. Do you not see that your saying 'I passed by Zaid' means my passing attached to Zaid? Mentioned in other copies of the principles that what ba enters upon is the object of attachment and the other side is the attached, as in your saying 'I wrote with the pen', meaning the writing attached to the pen. The answer: because the objective is to convey the action to the noun, not vice versa; the objective of your saying 'I wrote with the pen', 'I cut with the knife', and 'I struck with the sword' and similar examples is attachment of these actions to these things without the reverse, so the attached (action) is the original and the object of attachment is the follower like a tool for the thing. For this reason ba accompanies the prices in sales, because the price is not intended in the sale. So in 'al-Miftah'. [Commentary on 'and on this we said'] i.e., on the basis that what ba enters upon is the price. We said: in cases where the two counterparts in a sale are not monetary, both can serve as sold item and price; whichever side ba enters upon is the price and the other side is the sold item. So said some.

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal implication of the preposition ba (ب) for attachment (ilṣāq) in contracts of exchange such as sale. The author first states the rule: ba indicates that the item it enters upon is the price (thaman) and is a follower (tabaʿ) attached to the original (aṣl) which is the sold item (mabīʿ). This is proven by linguistic usage: in 'I cut with the knife' the cutting is attached to the knife, making the knife a tool and a follower. Similarly, in sale, the price is not the primary objective but a means to acquire the sold item, so it is a follower. Therefore, when ba enters upon a commodity in a sale contract, that commodity is deemed the price, and the other party is the sold item. An example: 'I sold you this slave for a kurr of wheat' – the slave is sold, the wheat is price, so the wheat can be substituted before delivery. If the statement is reversed, 'I sold you a kurr of wheat for this slave', then the wheat becomes the sold item and the slave the price, making the contract a salam (advance payment) which must be deferred. The commentary further explains the reasoning and resolves a potential objection: some might think the item with ba is the original (aṣl) because ba brings the verb to it, but since the verb's action is intended for the object (the thing acted upon), the object is the original and the tool is the follower, so ba enters upon the follower.

Key Terms
الباء (al-bāʾ): The Arabic letter ba used to indicate attachment (ilṣāq); in legal sales, it marks the price as a follower.
الإلصاق (al-ilṣāq): Attachment; the function of the letter ba to affix one thing to another.
تابع (tābiʿ): Follower; the item designated as price in a sale because it is a means to the primary object.

In Hanafi sales law, the preposition bā marks the price as a follower; its presence determines which item is the price and affects the contract's legal consequences.

The Letter Ba for Attachment: Linguistic Function and Legal Effect
Explaining the effect of the letter ba in conditional oaths and divorces

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the letter ba indicates attachment (ilṣāq) versus absolute condition, and derive the ruling for conditional oaths and divorces based on this distinction.

Core Idea
The ba for attachment ties the condition to a truthful event; without it, any report satisfies the condition.

The letter ba (باء) is a versatile particle in Arabic with multiple functions. In Hanafi usul, one critical function is 'ba al-ilṣāq' (the ba for attachment). This lesson explores how the presence or absence of this ba changes the legal outcome of conditional statements in oaths and divorces, as illustrated by examples from the Hanafi masters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the function of ba
In the given statement, determine whether the ba is present and if it is used for attachment. Look for constructions like 'إن أخبرتني بقدوم فلان' where ba connects the informing to the arrival.
Ba for attachment is distinct from ba for causation (sababiyya).
2
Apply the Hanafi principle
If ba is for attachment, the condition requires a truthful correspondence between the inform and the actual event. If ba is absent or used differently, the condition is based on absolute news.
The principle is: al-bā' lil-ilṣāq tuḍīfu al-khabar ilā al-wāqiʿ.
3
Derive the ruling
Based on the presence/absence of ba, state the legal consequence: false report frees or does not free; permission requirement per departure, etc.
Use the examples from the source to verify your derivation.
4
Note practical implications
Understand how this linguistic nuance affects divorce and manumission cases, and how it differs from other uses of ba.
This rule is unique to Hanafi usul and may differ from other madhhabs.

فلا يصح الاستبدال به قبل القبض.

So it is not valid to substitute with it before taking possession.

The master.

It is not valid except deferred. And our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) said: If he says to his slave: 'If you inform me of the arrival of so-and-so, you are free,' then that is based on truthful news, so that the news is attached to the arrival. So if he informs falsely, he is not freed. And if he says: 'If you inform me that so-and-so has arrived, you are free,' then that is based on absolute news. So if he informs falsely, he is freed. And if he says to his wife: 'If you leave the house except with my permission, then you are such-and-such,' she needs permission every time, because what is excluded is from it.

That is, her husband's permission.

So it becomes an event.

A departure attached to permission. So if she goes out a second time without permission, she is divorced. And if it is a subdivision of his statement 'she needs...'

Infinitive: i.e., except for a single departure with my permission.

The woman due to the existence of the condition, which is leaving without his permission.

He said: 'If you leave the house except that I give you permission,' then that is based on permission once, such that if after the first departure she goes out another time without permission, she is not divorced.

It occurs. For her with the departure.

One.

Due to the absence of the condition because the oath ends with one permission.

She goes out another time without permission, she is not divorced. And in al-Ziyadat: if he says: 'You are divorced by the will of Allah' or 'by the desire of Allah' or 'by His decree,' she is not divorced.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is an usul rule explaining the meaning of the letter ba (باء) for attachment (الإلصاق) and its legal consequences in conditional statements. The Hanafi scholars discuss several examples: First, when a master says to his slave 'If you inform me of the arrival of so-and-so, you are free' using the ba, the ba indicates attachment of the news to the arrival, requiring truthful news; false news does not free. Second, if the master says 'If you inform me that so-and-so has arrived' without the ba, the condition is based on absolute news, so even a false report frees the slave. Third, when a husband says to his wife 'If you leave the house except with my permission, then you are divorced,' the ba indicates that each departure requires separate permission; if she goes out a second time without permission, she is divorced. Fourth, if he says 'except that I give you permission,' the ba is not used, so once permission is given for a single departure, further departures without permission do not trigger divorce. Finally, if he says 'You are divorced by the will of Allah' using the ba, the divorce is suspended on the unknown will of Allah, so it does not occur. The commentary in the hashia explains that ba for attachment implies a temporal order, making it like a condition, whereas ba for causation would imply simultaneity. Hanafi usul uses these linguistic nuances to derive precise legal rulings.

Key Terms
الباء للإلصاق (al-bā' lil-ilṣāq): The letter ba used to denote attachment and connection between two things, implying a temporal order.
الإلصاق (al-ilṣāq): Attachment; the quality of being attached or connected, here used for the particle ba's function.
الخبر الصادق (al-khabar al-ṣādiq): Truthful news; news that matches reality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The slave is freed because the condition is met.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distinction of ba for attachment from other uses of ba is essential for correctly deriving rulings in conditional statements, requiring precise attention to the particle's function.
