النفس أيضاً غير ثابت وإلا يلزم تخلّف الحكم عن العلّة وهو باطل، كذا في بعض الحواشي. 
(۱) قوله: [قلنا بل حرمة إتلاف الطرف] ولقائل أن يقول: إن قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَن دَخَلَهُ كَانَ آمِناً [آل عمران : ٩٧] يدلّ على ثبوت حرمة النفس والأطراف جميعاً، فبأي دليل يترك الشافعي النص في النفس والأطراف وعلماؤنا في الأطراف، وأجيب: بأن الشافعي رحمه الله ترك لقوله عليــــه الــصـلاة والسلام: «الحرم لا يعيذ عاصياً ولا فاراً بدم»، وقال علماؤنا النص يتناول الأنفس دون الأطراف؛ لأنّ الأطراف في حكم الأموال على ما عرف، كذا قيل. 
(۲) قوله: [بعد إن كان حجةً] أي: ذلك الوصف حجة للمعلّل وشاهد له، وهذا كقلب الجراب وهـو جعل ظهره بطناً وبطنه ظهراً، فأي الوصف كان شاهداً عليك قلبته فجعلته شاهداً لك كان ظهــره إليك فصار وجهه إليك، وهذا النوع من القلب لا يتحقق إلا بوصف زائد فيه تفسير الوصف الأول لا مبدل له؛ لأنّ الوصف الواحد لا يمكن أن يكون شاهداً لحكمين متنافيين بدون الزيادة، كذا في "المعدن". 
(۳) قوله: [لَمّا كان الصوم... آه] قلتُ: ليس هذا جعل دليل المعلل وعلّته دليلاً لنا وعلّةً لحكمنا بعـــدم التعيين، بل بعد ضمّ أمر زائد إليه، هو تعيّن اليوم في نفسه للصوم ، وهذا ليس عكساً حقيقيا بــل صوريّاً، ثُمّ اعلم أنّ كلّ هذا المقاولات وأنحاء النظر والمحاورات مبنية على الغفلة ومطارحه قبل تنقيح محلّ البحث وتحقيق مضمون المدعى، فإنّه ينقح أوّلاً أنّ الحكم المعلّل بالفرضية هل هو وجوب مطلــــق التعيين فمسلّم وغير ضار؛ لأنه تعيين شرعي أو وجوب خصوص التعيين العبدي فضار، لكنـــه غير مسلّم اقتضاء العلة له، فبعد تنقيح لا يبقى للسائل إلا مقام المنع أو النقض أو المعارضة، كما حققه
Chapter on Directed Questions. That is, this type of qalb: he makes what the mu'allil made an 'illa for the ruling into a ma'lul for that ruling. Its example in the Shari'ah is the true inversion. Riba. That which is less than half a sa'. The occurrence of riba in much necessitates its occurrence in little, like coined money, so selling [like the Shafi'is say] of food that is measured. It is not as you say. Of food. The handful. From riba. Food with two handfuls of it. We said: No, rather the occurrence of riba in little necessitates its occurrence in much, like coined money. Similarly in the issue of the refugee in the Haram: the prohibition of destroying life and clothing, and both go back to one thing: changing the form of something contrary to the form it was on. Likewise in qiyas, the correct qalb is used in two meanings, and both go back to one meaning: changing the evidence to a form contrary to what it was on, as was said. (1) His saying: [ma'lulan for that ruling... etc.] means: what the mu'allil made an 'illa, the questioner makes a ma'lul, i.e., a ruling, and what the mu'allil made a ma'lul, the questioner makes an 'illa. In that is nullification of the ta'lil by nullifying its 'illa by making it a ruling. And this type of qalb is only valid when the arguer made ta'lil by the ruling, i.e., he made what was a ruling in the original into an 'illa for another ruling. As for if he made ta'lil by a pure description, this qalb does not apply to it because a description cannot become a ruling in any way, nor can the established ruling become an 'illa for it because it precedes the ruling, as in "al-Ma'dan". (2) His saying: [with two handfuls of it] except for one who made riba in much forbidden in the sale of what is subject to measurement necessitates the occurrence of riba in little like coined money, for riba occurs in both little and much, and the common element is that riba occurs in both. (3) His saying: [Rather the occurrence of riba... etc.] i.e., we have inverted the opponent's ta'lil and made its occurrence in much a ruling, which was an 'illa in the opponent's qiyas, and made its occurrence in little an 'illa, which was a ruling in his qiyas. In that, qalb is only in the original, and the author (may Allah have mercy on him) made qalb in the branch, and it is answered that qalb in the branch necessitates qalb in the original, as in "al-Ma'dan". (4) His saying: [The refugee in the Haram, and he is one against whom there is qisas for life, for if he takes refuge in the Haram, he is not killed therein according to us because of His saying: "and whoever enters it is secure" [Al 'Imran: 97], except that he is not fed, not given drink, not sat with, not sold to until he is forced to go out, then he is killed outside the Haram. According to al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him), he is killed in the Haram by qiyas on one against whom there is qisas for a limb, because if he takes refuge in the Haram, qisas is exacted from him by agreement, so likewise qisas for life, and the common element is that each is a criminal. The companions of al-Shafi'i said: The prohibition of destroying life necessitates the prohibition of destroying a limb, i.e., the prohibition of destroying life is an 'illa for the prohibition of destroying a limb, like game of the Haram; the prohibition of its life necessitates the prohibition of its limb. And the prohibition of the limb is not established for the refugee by consensus, so it is known that the prohibition [of life is not established either]. Chapter on Directed Questions. From the limbs. Necessitates the prohibition of destroying the limb like game. We said: Rather the prohibition of destroying the limb necessitates the prohibition of destroying life like game. So when you make its 'illa ma'lulan for that ruling, it does not remain an 'illa for it because it is impossible for one thing to be both an 'illa and a ma'lul for something without considering different aspects. And the second type of qalb is that the questioner makes what the mu'allil made an 'illa for what he claimed of the ruling into an 'illa for the opposite of that ruling, so it becomes evidence for the questioner after it was evidence for the mu'allil. Its example and witness: Fasting. In invalidating the mu'allil's qiyas by way of qalb. Ramadan fasting is an obligatory fast, so specification is required for it like make-up fasting. We said: Since the fast was obligatory [like make-up fasting, but specification is not required because the day itself is specified]. The life (?) is also not established, otherwise the absence of the ruling from the 'illa would be necessary, and that is invalid, as in some marginal notes. (1) His saying: [We said: Rather the prohibition of destroying the limb] and someone might say that His saying: "and whoever enters it is secure" [Al 'Imran: 97] indicates the establishment of the prohibition of life and limbs all together, so by what evidence does al-Shafi'i leave the text in life and limbs, and our scholars in limbs? And it was answered: al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) left it because of his (peace and blessings be upon him) saying: "The Haram does not grant refuge to a wrongdoer nor to a fleer from blood", and our scholars said the text covers lives without limbs because limbs are considered as property, as is known. (2) His saying: [after it was evidence] i.e., that description was evidence for the mu'allil and witness for him. This is like turning over a bag, making its back into its belly and its belly into its back. So whichever description was a witness against you, you turned it and made it a witness for you; its back was towards you so its face became towards you. This type of qalb cannot be realized except with an additional description that explains the first description, not a substitute for it, because one description cannot be a witness for two conflicting rulings without the addition, as in "al-Ma'dan". (3) His saying: [Since the fast was... etc.] I said: This is not making the mu'allil's evidence and 'illa evidence for us and an 'illa for our ruling of lack of specification, but rather after adding an additional matter to it, which is the specification of the day itself for fasting. And this is not a true inversion but a formal one. Then know that all these discussions and types of consideration and dialogues are based on heedlessness and conjecture before refining the locus of inquiry and verifying the content of the claim. For it is first refined: the ruling made 'illa by obligatoriness—is it an absolute obligation of specification? Then it is conceded and not harmful because it is a legal specification. Or is it an obligation of particular servant specification? Then it is harmful, but its being required by the 'illa is not conceded. So after refinement, the questioner only has the position of denial, objection, or opposition, as he verified.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Usul al-Shashi discusses two types of qalb (inversion) used in debate (munazara). Qalb is a method to turn the opponent's proof against them. The first type (true inversion) involves swapping the roles of 'illa (cause) and ma'lul (ruling). For example, Shafi'is argued that the occurrence of riba in large quantities of food necessitates its occurrence in small quantities, like currency. The Hanafi response uses qalb: instead, the occurrence in small quantities necessitates occurrence in large quantities, thus invalidating the Shafi'i analogy. The second type (formal inversion) involves taking the opponent's 'illa and using it to support the opposite ruling, but this often requires an additional attribute. An example is the debate over fasting in Ramadan: Shafi'is said Ramadan fast is obligatory and therefore requires specification (niyyah) like make-up fasts; Hanafis responded that the very day itself is specified, so no additional specification is needed. The commentary notes that proper qalb must be distinguished from mere sophistry, and that many debates arise from not refining the point of contention first. The passage also includes a detailed discussion of the case of a criminal seeking refuge in the Haram, where Hanafis and Shafi'is differed on whether the death penalty can be executed there.

Key Terms
القلب (al-qalb): Turning the opponent's evidence against them by inverting the relationship between 'illa and ma'lul or using the 'illa for the opposite ruling
المعلل (al-mu'allil): The one who provides the 'illa (cause) for a ruling in argumentation
المعلول (al-ma'lul): The ruling that is caused by the 'illa

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key condition for type 1 qalb to be valid?
A) The 'illa must be a pure description.
B) The 'illa must be a ruling (hukm).
C) The 'illa must be based on a Quranic text.
D) The 'illa must be agreed upon by consensus.

Key Takeaway
Type 1 qalb inverts the opponent's 'illa and ma'lul, but only works when the 'illa is a ruling; it is a powerful tool in Hanafi debate.

Type 2 of Qalb: Formal Inversion (Qalb Suri)
Explaining type 2 qalb with the fasting example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish type 2 qalb from type 1 and apply it to refute an opponent's argument by using their own 'illa for the opposite ruling, provided an additional attribute is present.

Core Idea
Type 2 qalb turns the opponent's 'illa into evidence for the opposite ruling, but only with an extra attribute; it is not a true inversion.

The second type of qalb is more subtle: it retains the opponent's 'illa but uses it to prove the opposite ruling by adding a new attribute. This lesson explores the example of fasting in Ramadan to illustrate this technique. We will also see how this differs from type 1.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's ta'lil and their 'illa
Determine the 'illa the opponent uses and the ruling they aim to prove.
In the fasting example, the Shafi'i 'illa is 'obligatoriness of the fast' and the ruling is 'specification (niyyah) is required'.
2
Determine if the 'illa can be used for the opposite ruling with an additional attribute
Check whether adding a new attribute (e.g., the day's predetermination) changes the direction of the 'illa's implication.
The additional attribute must explain why the original 'illa now supports the opposite.
3
Add the attribute and derive the opposite ruling
State the additional attribute and show how the 'illa now proves the opposite ruling.
The Hanafi argument: the day itself is already specified, so no extra specification is needed; the obligatoriness actually supports the opposite.
4
Distinguish from type 1
Note that type 2 does not swap 'illa and ma'lul; it adds a new element to change the implication.
This is like turning a bag inside out, not swapping its contents.

(۱) قوله: [معلولاً لذلك الحكم... إلخ] يعني: ما جعله المعلّل علة جعله السائل معلولاً أي: حكماً ومــــا جعله المعلل معلولاً جعله السائل علة، وفيه إبطال التعليل بإبطال علّته بجعلها حكماً، وإنما يصح هذا النوع من القلب فيما إذا علّل المستدلّ بالحكم بأن جعل ما كان حكماً في الأصل علة لحكم آخر، فأمّا لو علّل بالوصف المحض لا يرد عليه هذا القلب؛ لأنّ الوصف لا يصير حكماً بوجه ولا يصير الحكـــــم الثابت علّةً له؛ لأنه سابق على الحكم، كذا في "المعدن".

(۳) قوله: [بل جريان الربا... إلخ] أي: فقد قلبنا تعليل الخصم وجعلنا جريانه في الكثير حكماً وهو علّة في قياس الخصم وجعلنا جريانه في القليل علّة وهو حكم في قياسه، وفيه أنّ القلب إنما يكون في الأصل، والمصنف رحمه قلب في الفرع ويجاب : بأنّ القلب في الفرع يستلزم القلب في الأصل، كذا في "المعدن".

Riba. That which is less than half a sa'. The occurrence of riba in much necessitates its occurrence in little, like coined money, so selling [like the Shafi'is say] of food that is measured. It is not as you say. Of food. The handful. From riba. Food with two handfuls of it. We said: No, rather the occurrence of riba in little necessitates its occurrence in much, like coined money. Similarly in the issue of the refugee in the Haram: the prohibition of destroying life and clothing, and both go back to one thing: changing the form of something contrary to the form it was on. Likewise in qiyas, the correct qalb is used in two meanings, and both go back to one meaning: changing the evidence to a form contrary to what it was on, as was said. (1) His saying: [ma'lulan for that ruling... etc.] means: what the mu'allil made an 'illa, the questioner makes a ma'lul, i.e., a ruling, and what the mu'allil made a ma'lul, the questioner makes an 'illa. In that is nullification of the ta'lil by nullifying its 'illa by making it a ruling. And this type of qalb is only valid when the arguer made ta'lil by the ruling, i.e., he made what was a ruling in the original into an 'illa for another ruling. As for if he made ta'lil by a pure description, this qalb does not apply to it because a description cannot become a ruling in any way, nor can the established ruling become an 'illa for it because it precedes the ruling, as in "al-Ma'dan". (2) His saying: [with two handfuls of it] except for one who made riba in much forbidden in the sale of what is subject to measurement necessitates the occurrence of riba in little like coined money, for riba occurs in both little and much, and the common element is that riba occurs in both. (3) His saying: [Rather the occurrence of riba... etc.] i.e., we have inverted the opponent's ta'lil and made its occurrence in much a ruling, which was an 'illa in the opponent's qiyas, and made its occurrence in little an 'illa, which was a ruling in his qiyas. In that, qalb is only in the original, and the author (may Allah have mercy on him) made qalb in the branch, and it is answered that qalb in the branch necessitates qalb in the original, as in "al-Ma'dan". (4) His saying: [The refugee in the Haram, and he is one against whom there is qisas for life, for if he takes refuge in the Haram, he is not killed therein according to us because of His saying: "and whoever enters it is secure" [Al 'Imran: 97], except that he is not fed, not given drink, not sat with, not sold to until he is forced to go out, then he is killed outside the Haram. According to al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him), he is killed in the Haram by qiyas on one against whom there is qisas for a limb, because if he takes refuge in the Haram, qisas is exacted from him by agreement, so likewise qisas for life, and the common element is that each is a criminal. The companions of al-Shafi'i said: The prohibition of destroying life necessitates the prohibition of destroying a limb, i.e., the prohibition of destroying life is an 'illa for the prohibition of destroying a limb, like game of the Haram; the prohibition of its life necessitates the prohibition of its limb. And the prohibition of the limb is not established for the refugee by consensus, so it is known that the prohibition [of life is not established either]. Chapter on Directed Questions. From the limbs. Necessitates the prohibition of destroying the limb like game. We said: Rather the prohibition of destroying the limb necessitates the prohibition of destroying life like game. So when you make its 'illa ma'lulan for that ruling, it does not remain an 'illa for it because it is impossible for one thing to be both an 'illa and a ma'lul for something without considering different aspects. And the second type of qalb is that the questioner makes what the mu'allil made an 'illa for what he claimed of the ruling into an 'illa for the opposite of that ruling, so it becomes evidence for the questioner after it was evidence for the mu'allil. Its example and witness: Fasting. In invalidating the mu'allil's qiyas by way of qalb. Ramadan fasting is an obligatory fast, so specification is required for it like make-up fasting. We said: Since the fast was obligatory [like make-up fasting, but specification is not required because the day itself is specified]. The life (?) is also not established, otherwise the absence of the ruling from the 'illa would be necessary, and that is invalid, as in some marginal notes. (1) His saying: [We said: Rather the prohibition of destroying the limb] and someone might say that His saying: "and whoever enters it is secure" [Al 'Imran: 97] indicates the establishment of the prohibition of life and limbs all together, so by what evidence does al-Shafi'i leave the text in life and limbs, and our scholars in limbs? And it was answered: al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) left it because of his (peace and blessings be upon him) saying: "The Haram does not grant refuge to a wrongdoer nor to a fleer from blood", and our scholars said the text covers lives without limbs because limbs are considered as property, as is known. (2) His saying: [after it was evidence] i.e., that description was evidence for the mu'allil and witness for him. This is like turning over a bag, making its back into its belly and its belly into its back. So whichever description was a witness against you, you turned it and made it a witness for you; its back was towards you so its face became towards you. This type of qalb cannot be realized except with an additional description that explains the first description, not a substitute for it, because one description cannot be a witness for two conflicting rulings without the addition, as in "al-Ma'dan". (3) His saying: [Since the fast was... etc.] I said: This is not making the mu'allil's evidence and 'illa evidence for us and an 'illa for our ruling of lack of specification, but rather after adding an additional matter to it, which is the specification of the day itself for fasting. And this is not a true inversion but a formal one. Then know that all these discussions and types of consideration and dialogues are based on heedlessness and conjecture before refining the locus of inquiry and verifying the content of the claim. For it is first refined: the ruling made 'illa by obligatoriness—is it an absolute obligation of specification? Then it is conceded and not harmful because it is a legal specification. Or is it an obligation of particular servant specification? Then it is harmful, but its being required by the 'illa is not conceded. So after refinement, the questioner only has the position of denial, objection, or opposition, as he verified.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Usul al-Shashi discusses two types of qalb (inversion) used in debate (munazara). Qalb is a method to turn the opponent's proof against them. The first type (true inversion) involves swapping the roles of 'illa (cause) and ma'lul (ruling). For example, Shafi'is argued that the occurrence of riba in large quantities of food necessitates its occurrence in small quantities, like currency. The Hanafi response uses qalb: instead, the occurrence in small quantities necessitates occurrence in large quantities, thus invalidating the Shafi'i analogy. The second type (formal inversion) involves taking the opponent's 'illa and using it to support the opposite ruling, but this often requires an additional attribute. An example is the debate over fasting in Ramadan: Shafi'is said Ramadan fast is obligatory and therefore requires specification (niyyah) like make-up fasts; Hanafis responded that the very day itself is specified, so no additional specification is needed. The commentary notes that proper qalb must be distinguished from mere sophistry, and that many debates arise from not refining the point of contention first. The passage also includes a detailed discussion of the case of a criminal seeking refuge in the Haram, where Hanafis and Shafi'is differed on whether the death penalty can be executed there.

Key Terms
القلب الصوري (al-qalb al-suri): Formal inversion: using the opponent's 'illa for the opposite ruling by adding an additional attribute.
الوصف الزائد (al-wasf al-za'id): The additional attribute that changes the implication of the 'illa in type 2 qalb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is required for type 2 qalb to be valid?
A) The 'illa must be a ruling.
B) An additional attribute must be added to the 'illa.
C) The 'illa must be a pure description.
D) The opponent must be a Shafi'i.

Key Takeaway
Type 2 qalb uses the opponent's 'illa but adds an extra attribute to derive the opposite ruling; it is a sophisticated rebuttal requiring careful identification of the additional element.

Distinguishing Sabab, 'Illa, Shart, and 'Alama
Defining four categories of attachment of rulings in Hanafi usul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sabab, 'illa, shart, and 'alama according to the Hanafi definition and apply these categories to a given legal scenario.

Core Idea
Sabab leads via an intermediary; 'illa has inherent effect; shart is a condition; 'alama is a sign.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, rulings are attached to certain causes and conditions. Understanding the precise definitions of sabab (cause as means), 'illa (effective cause), shart (condition), and 'alama (sign) is essential for correct legal reasoning. This lesson from al-Shashi's Usul clarifies these four categories with a concrete example and a Quranic proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four terms
From the passage, extract the definitions of sabab, 'illa, shart, and 'alama. Note how each relates to the ruling.
The passage uses the example of opening a stable door as a sabab.
2
Understand the key distinction
Sabab has 'ifḍā' (conduciveness) but no 'iqtiḍā' (inherent force); 'illa has both. The ruling is attributed to 'illa when both are present.
This distinction is critical in Hanafi istinbat.
3
Apply the definitions to the example
Using the stable example, identify the sabab (opening the door), the intermediary (exit of the animal), and the 'illa (the act of opening that directly causes loss).
The ruling of liability is attributed to the 'illa, not the sabab.
4
Contrast shart and 'alama
Shart is necessary for the ruling's existence but is not a cause; 'alama merely indicates the ruling without any causal role.
For instance, the sighting of the moon is an 'alama for Ramadan, not a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل الحكم يتعلق بسببه ويثبت بعلته ويوجد عند شرطه. فالسبب ما يكون طريقاً إلى الشيء بواسطة. قال تعالى: {وآتيناه من كل شيء سبباً}. ففتح باب الإصطبل والقفص وحل قيد العبد سبب للتلف بواسطة خروج الدابة والطير والعبد. والسبب مع العلة إذا اجتمعا يضاف الحكم إلى العلة. والحاصل أن ما فيه إفضاء لا اقتضاء هو السبب، وما فيه اقتضاء للحكم أيضاً بذاته هو العلة، وما ليس فيه إفضاء ولا اقتضاء بل لوجوده دخل في تحقق الحكم هو الشرط، وما ليس له دخل بل مجرد تعريف وكشف هو العلامة.
Chapter: The ruling is attached to its cause, established by its effective cause, and exists at its condition. So the sabab is what is a means to a thing through an intermediary. Allah said: 'And We gave him from everything a cause.' So opening the door of the stable and cage and untying the slave's shackles is a cause for destruction through the intermediary of the animal, bird, and slave leaving. When sabab and 'illa are together, the ruling is attributed to the 'illa. The summary: What has conduciveness without effective force is the sabab; what has effective force for the ruling in itself is the 'illa; what has neither but its existence is necessary for the ruling's realization is the shart; what has no role but mere definition and disclosure is the 'alama.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a classic usul al-fiqh definition of the four things to which rulings are attached: sabab (cause as a means), 'illa (effective cause), shart (condition), and 'alama (sign). The author begins by stating that a ruling is attached to its sabab, established by its 'illa, and exists at its shart. Then he defines sabab as a means to something through an intermediary, giving the example of opening a stable or cage leading to the loss of animals through the intermediary of their exiting. He cites the Quranic verse (Surah al-Kahf 18:84) to illustrate the linguistic usage. He notes that when both sabab and 'illa are present, the ruling is attributed to the 'illa because it is the effective cause. The summary provides clear distinctions: Sabab is a conduit without inherent force; 'illa has inherent force to bring about the ruling; shart is a necessary prerequisite without which the ruling does not exist but which is not a cause; and 'alama is merely a sign indicating the ruling. This classification is fundamental in Hanafi usul for understanding how rulings are derived and applied.

Key Terms
السبب (al-sabab): A means that leads to a ruling through an intermediary (wasita).
العلة (al-illa): The effective cause that inherently brings about the ruling.
الشرط (al-shart): A condition whose existence is necessary for the ruling but not a cause.
العلامة (al-alama): A sign that indicates the ruling without having any causal role.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes sabab from 'illa?
A) Sabab has inherent effectiveness, while 'illa does not.
B) Sabab leads to the ruling through an intermediary, while 'illa has inherent effect on the ruling.
C) 'Illa is a condition, while sabab is a sign.
D) Both are the same; the terms are interchangeable.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, sabab is a means through an intermediary, 'illa has inherent effect, shart is a necessary condition, and 'alama is a sign; the ruling is attributed to the 'illa when both sabab and 'illa are present.

Ruling Attached to the Legislated Cause: Sleep, Khalwa, Travel
Explaining the principle that ruling attaches to the legislated cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle that the ruling is attached to the shar‘ī cause (sabab) rather than the actual cause, and apply it to the examples of sleep, khalwa, and travel.

Core Idea
The sharī'ah attaches rulings to apparent causes, not hidden realities, to ensure practical enforceability.

In this passage from Uṣūl al-Shāshī, the author establishes a foundational principle: the ruling in sharī'ah is tied to the cause (sabab) as defined by the Lawgiver, not necessarily the actual physical event. This is explained through three key examples—sleep, valid seclusion (khalwa ṣaḥīḥa), and travel—where the sharī'ah substitutes a clear, observable indicator for a hidden reality due to the difficulty of ascertaining the latter. Understanding this principle is critical for correctly applying many fiqh rulings, especially where the actual cause is unverifiable.

Guided Path
1
Identify Actual Cause and Shar‘ī Cause
For each example (sleep, khalwa, travel), determine what the actual cause is (ḥadath, waṭ’, mashaqqa) and what the shar‘ī cause is (nawm, khalwa ṣaḥīḥa, safar).
2
Understand the Rationale
Recognize that the shar‘ī cause is substituted because the actual cause is difficult to ascertain reliably in practice (ta‘adhdhur al-iṭṭilā‘).
This is a form of istiḥsān.
3
Analyze the Substitution Mechanism
Observe how the shar‘ah 'establishes in place' (iqāmat al-maqām) so that the ruling is attached to the substitute, ignoring the actual reality.
4
Apply the Principle to a New Scenario
Consider: A person sleeps but is certain no impurity was emitted. Does wuḍū’ break? According to the principle, yes, because sleep is the shar‘ī cause.
This applies even without actual ḥadath.
5
Connect to Broader Uṣūl Framework
Note that this is a type of istiḥsān, where the jurist deviates from strict qiyās (which would require actual cause) to facilitate the law's application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل الحكم يتعلق بسببه
أي مثل إقامة النوم مقام الحدث. اعتبار حقيقة الحدث ويدار الانتقاض على كمال النوم، وكذلك الخلوة لتعذر الاطلاع على الوطئ. الصحيحة لما أقيمت مقام الوطئ سقط اعتبار حقيقة الوطئ فيدار الحكم على حيث يلزم بها ولو لم يطأها. صحة الخلوة في حق كمال المهر ولزوم العدة، وكذلك السفر لما أقيم مقام المشقة في حق الرخصة سقط اعتبار حقيقة المشقة ويدار الحكم على نفس السفر. أن السلطان لو طاف في أطراف مملكته يقصد به مقدار السفر كان له الرخصة، وهو سير ثلاثة أيام ولياليها سيرا وسطا في الرباعي. في الإفطار والقصر وقد يسمى الوضوء كان ثابتا بيقين، وفي النوم خروج النجاسة مشكوك؛ لأن الشارع أقام نفس النوم مقام حقيقة خروج النجاسة.
قوله: [ وكذلك الخلوة... إلخ] أي: إذا خلا الزوج بامرأة وليس هناك مانع من الوطي لا شرعا ولا حسا ولا طبعا كصوم هو مانع وطي شرعا، ومرض هو مانع وطي حسا، وحيض هو مانع وطي طبعا وشرعا، كأنه وطئها إقامة للخلوة الصحيحة مقام الوطي، ولذا سقط اعتبار حقيقة الوطي ويدار الحكم هو وجوب المهر والعدة وغيرهما على صحة الخلوة بأن لم يوجد مانع فيجب المهر الكامل ويلزم العدة وإن يتيقن أنه ما كان بينهما وطي بأن كانت بكرا بعد الخلوة، فإن قلت: تعذر الاطلاع على الوطي لهما ممنوع حتى لو توافقا على انتفاء الوطي يجب أن لا يحكم بلزوم المهر والعدة، قلت: جاز أن يكون توافقهما تواضعا منهما لمصلحة من المصالح بأن كان لهما مصلحة في بكارتها ليرغب غير الزوج الأول إليها، وكذا للزوج دفع تهمة اللعنة وغيرها فتحقق تعذر الاطلاع في حق أحكام الشرع والناس وغيرهما لم يشهدوا عندهما، كذا في 'الفصول' وغيره.
Chapter: The ruling is attached to its cause. That is, like establishing sleep in place of the event (ḥadath). Consideration of the reality of the event, and annulment (of wuḍū') is made to revolve around complete sleep. Similarly, (valid) seclusion (khalwa) because of the difficulty of ascertaining intercourse. The valid (seclusion) when established in place of intercourse, consideration of the reality of intercourse falls away, and the ruling is made to revolve around it such that (the obligations) are necessitated by it even if he did not have intercourse with her. The validity of seclusion regarding the full mahr and the necessity of the 'iddah. Likewise, travel when established in place of hardship regarding the concession (rukḥṣa), consideration of the reality of hardship falls away and the ruling is made to revolve around the travel itself. That the sultan if he tours the outskirts of his kingdom intending by it the distance of travel, he has the concession—travel is three days and their nights, moderate walking, for shortening (the four-unit prayers) and breaking the fast. And it is sometimes said: wuḍū' was established with certainty, and in sleep the emission of impurity is doubtful, because the Lawgiver established sleep itself in place of the reality of impurity emission. His saying: [And likewise seclusion... etc.] Meaning: when a husband secludes with a woman and there is no impediment to intercourse, neither shar'ī, nor sensory, nor natural—such as fasting which is a shar'ī impediment, illness which is a sensory impediment, menstruation which is a natural and shar'ī impediment—it is as if he had intercourse with her, because valid seclusion is established in place of intercourse. Therefore the reality of intercourse is not considered, and the ruling—which is the obligation of the full mahr and the 'iddah and other things—is made to revolve around the validity of seclusion: if there is no impediment, the full mahr is obligatory and the 'iddah is necessary even if it is certain that there was no intercourse between them, e.g., if she was a virgin after the seclusion. If you say: the difficulty of ascertaining intercourse between them is not granted; even if they agree on the absence of intercourse, it should not be judged that mahr and 'iddah are necessary. I say: it is possible that their agreement is a collusion between them for some interest, e.g., they have an interest in her virginity so that another besides the first husband may desire her, and likewise for the husband to avoid the accusation of divorce, etc. Thus the difficulty of ascertainment is realized in the rulings of sharī'ah and people, etc., for they do not have witnesses with them. Thus in 'al-Fuṣūl' and others.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage from the matn of Uṣūl al-Shāshī establishes a key uṣūl principle: the sharī'ah ruling is attached to the cause as defined by the Lawgiver, not necessarily to the actual physical reality. The commentator expands on three examples: (1) Sleep is established as the cause for the annulment of wuḍū', even though the actual cause is the emission of impurities; because it is difficult to ascertain whether such emission occurred, sleep substitutes it. (2) Valid seclusion (khalwa ṣaḥīḥa) between a husband and wife, when no impediment exists, is treated as the cause for the obligation of the full mahr and the 'iddah, even if actual intercourse did not occur. The commentator addresses a potential objection: if both parties agree that no intercourse took place, why impose these obligations? He explains that their agreement may be collusive, and since we cannot reliably ascertain the truth, the sharī'ah takes the apparent cause. (3) Travel is established as the cause for the concessions (rukhṣa) of shortening prayers and breaking fast, even if the traveler does not experience actual hardship. The minimum distance is three days' moderate travel. The principle is that the sharī'ah considers the generic cause (sleep, seclusion, travel) rather than the specific actuality, due to the difficulty of ascertaining the latter. This is a form of istiḥsān and taqsīr of the cause.

Key Terms
السبب (al-sabab): The cause to which the sharī'ah attaches a ruling, which may be a substitute for the actual cause.
إقامة المقام (iqāmat al-maqām): Establishing something in place of another as a substitute cause.
تعذر الاطلاع (ta‘adhdhur al-iṭṭilā‘): Difficulty or impossibility of ascertaining a hidden reality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the sharī'ah establish valid seclusion (khalwa ṣaḥīḥa) as the cause for the obligation of full mahr and ‘iddah, even if no intercourse occurred?
A) Because seclusion is a more reliable indicator than intercourse.
B) Because it is difficult to ascertain whether intercourse actually took place.
C) Because the couple would always lie about it.
D) Because seclusion is a form of worship.

Key Takeaway
The ruling is attached to the shar‘ī cause (sabab) defined by the Lawgiver, not the hidden reality, due to the difficulty of ascertaining the actual cause.

The Metaphorical Cause: Oaths and Conditional Divorce
Explains metaphorical cause with oaths and conditional divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a real cause and a metaphorical cause in Hanafi usul and apply this distinction to oaths and conditional divorce.

Core Idea
An oath is called a cause for expiation only metaphorically, as it actually prevents the violation that would require expiation.

This passage addresses a common objection: if expiation is attributed to an oath (kaffarat al-yamin), why isn't the oath a real cause? The author explains that a real cause does not conflict with its effect, but an oath is meant to prevent violation, so it conflicts with expiation. It is called a cause only metaphorically, considering its potential outcome if violated. The same applies to conditional divorce and manumission. The Quranic example of grapes being called wine illustrates naming something by its potential. Understanding this distinction prevents logical errors in identifying legal causes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Recognize that some consider an oath a real cause for expiation because expiation is attributed to it (e.g., 'kaffarat al-yamin').
2
Examine the nature of an oath
An oath is concluded for piety (birr) and to prevent violation (hanth); its purpose conflicts with the occurrence of hanth.
3
Contrast with a real cause
A real cause does not conflict with its effect. Here, if the oath were a real cause, expiation would be obligatory even without hanth, but it only becomes due upon hanth.
4
Apply to conditional divorce
Similarly, conditional divorce (e.g., 'if you enter the house, you are divorced') is meant to prevent entry, so it prevents the condition that triggers divorce. It is called a cause metaphorically.
5
Understand the Quranic analogy
In Surah Yusuf, 'I see myself pressing wine' refers to grapes, naming something by its potential. This justifies the metaphorical use of 'cause'.
Dalil block: the verse shows linguistic permissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يسمى غير السبب سببا مجازا كاليمين يسمى سببا للكفارة وإنها ليست بسبب في الحقيقة؛ لانعقادها للبر لا للحنث حتى يجب الكفارة. فإن السبب لا ينافي وجود المسبب واليمين ينافي وجوب الكفارة؛ فإن الكفارة إنما تجب بالحنث وبه ينتهي اليمين. وكذلك تعليق الحكم بالشرط كالطلاق بأن قال إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق، والعتاق يسمى سببا مجازا وأنه ليس بسبب في الحقيقة؛ لأن الحكم إنما يثبت لوقوع الطلاق أو العتاق. 
قوله: [وقد يسمى غير السبب ... إلخ] جواب نقض يرد على ما ذكره المصنف أولا وهو أن السبب ما يكون طريقا إلى الحكم مفضيا إليه واليمين سبب الكفارة، ولهذا يضاف إليه كما يقال كفارة اليمين أنه ليس بموصل إليهما، بل اليمين ينافي وجوب الكفارة؛ لأن الكفارة لا تجب إلا مع بالحنث، واليمين انعقد للبر و شرعت له والبر ينافي الحنث فكان اليمين مانعا للحنث، والحنث لازم الكفارة ومنافي اللازم مناف للملزوم. وكذلك تعليق الطلاق والعتاق يسمى سببا للطلاق والعتاق مع أنه مناف لهما؛ لأن قوله: «إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق» المقصود منه امتناعها دخول الدار حذرا عن الطلاق، فكان التعليق مانعا وجود الشرط وهو لازم للجزاء. فأجاب بأنها سميت سببا باعتبار ما يؤول إليه بأن خالف ولزم الكفارة والجزاء، كما يسمى العنب خمرا في قوله تعالى: {إني أراني أعصر خمرا} [يوسف: 36].
And sometimes what is not a cause is called a cause metaphorically, like the oath is called a cause for expiation (kaffāra) although it is not a cause in reality; because it is concluded for piety (birr) not for violation (ḥanth) so that expiation is obligatory. For the cause does not conflict with the existence of the effect, while the oath conflicts with the obligation of expiation; because expiation is only obligatory with violation, and by it the oath ends. Similarly, the conditioning of a ruling upon a condition, like divorce: if he says 'If you enter the house, you are divorced', and manumission ('itāq) is called a cause metaphorically, and it is not a cause in reality; because the ruling is only established by the occurrence of divorce or manumission. His saying: [And sometimes what is not a cause... etc.] is a response to a refutation directed at what the author mentioned earlier, which is that the cause is a path leading to the ruling, and the oath is a cause of expiation; therefore it is attributed to it, as it is said 'expiation of an oath'. It is not a means to them; rather the oath conflicts with the obligation of expiation; because expiation is only obligatory with violation, and the oath is concluded for piety and legislated for it, and piety conflicts with violation, so the oath is a preventer of violation, and violation is necessary for expiation, and that which conflicts with the necessary conflicts with the necessitated. Similarly, conditioning divorce and manumission is called a cause for divorce and manumission although it conflicts with them; because his saying 'If you enter the house, you are divorced' is intended to prevent her entering the house out of caution from divorce; thus the conditioning is a preventer of the existence of the condition, which is necessary for the consequence. So he answered that they are called cause by consideration of what they lead to, i.e., if he violates, expiation becomes necessary; just as grapes are called wine in the verse {Indeed I see myself pressing wine} [Yusuf: 36].
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection. Some may argue that an oath is a cause for expiation because expiation is attributed to it (kaffārat al-yamīn). The author responds by distinguishing between real cause and metaphorical cause. A real cause does not conflict with its effect; but an oath actually prevents the obligation of expiation because it is made to prevent violation. Expiation only becomes obligatory upon violation (ḥanth), which ends the oath. So the oath is not a cause; it is called a cause only metaphorically, considering its potential outcome (if violated, expiation becomes due). Similarly, conditional divorce (ta'līq al-talāq) is called a cause for divorce, but it actually prevents divorce because the condition is meant to be avoided. The example of grapes being called wine in the Qur'an (referring to what they will become) illustrates calling something by its potential result. The commentator explains the reasoning in full. This clarifies that in uṣūl al-fiqh, the term 'sabab' can be used in a metaphorical sense for things that are potential causes, but the actual cause is the violation or the occurrence of the condition.

Key Terms
السبب المجازي (al-sabab al-majāzī): A thing that is called a cause not literally but because it can lead to the ruling through an intermediary (like violation).
اليمين (al-yamīn): An oath; a solemn promise, often to do or refrain from an act, which requires expiation if broken.
الحنث (al-ḥanth): Violation of an oath; the act that makes expiation obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is conditional divorce (ta'līq al-ṭalāq) considered a metaphorical cause rather than a real cause?
A) Because the condition (entry into house) never occurs.
B) Because the condition is meant to be avoided, thus it prevents the divorce.
C) Because divorce is not a ruling but a personal action.
D) Because the condition is set by the wife, not the husband.

Key Takeaway
An oath and conditional divorce are called causes only metaphorically because they actually prevent the events that would lead to the ruling; the ruling follows only upon violation or condition occurrence.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

The Hanafi Definition of Sabab (Cause) in Usul
Definition of sabab (cause) with proof and dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'sabab', distinguish it from the concept of 'illah, and apply it to explain why time is considered the cause for prayer obligation.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, sabab is an amara (sign) that reveals the ruling, not the efficient cause which is God's eternal command.

In this lesson, we delve into the Hanafi usul definition of 'sabab' (cause) as presented in the classical text. The author explains that legal rulings are connected to external causes, but these causes are merely indicators (amarat) that inform the servant of the obligation, not true effectors. This distinction shapes the entire Hanafi approach to legal causation. We will explore the reasoning, the evidence from Quran and Sunnah, and the practical implications such as prayer obligation for the sleeper and unconscious person.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Read the opening statement: 'Legal rulings are connected to their causes.' The term 'sabab' is the focus. Determine what the passage is defining.
The passage clarifies that sabab is not the real cause (which is God's command) but an external sign.
2
Extract the Hanafi definition
Locate where the text explicitly says 'causes here mean signs and identifiers that reveal rulings, not in the sense of effectors.' This is the core definition.
The Hanafi definition: sabab is an amara (sign) and mu'arraf (identifier), not mu'aththir (effector).
3
Analyze the proof (dalil)
The passage cites Quran 17:78 (lam of causality in 'liduluk') and the case of the sleeper/non-conscious person. How does the text use these to support the definition?
The lam in 'liduluk' is argued to indicate causality, but the commentary clarifies it is a sign, not a necessitating cause.
4
Contrast with the opposing school
The text mentions that the mashayikh chose the word 'sabab' over 'illah' because it is more general. This hints at a difference with the Mutakallimin. Identify the opposing view and the Hanafi response.
Mutakallimin may treat sabab as an efficient cause analogous to 'illah. Hanafis respond that if sabab were a true cause, performance would be impossible because cause precedes effect.
5
Apply the definition to a key example
The passage uses time as the sabab for prayer. Walk through the argument: time is a sign (amara) for obligation; obligation exists even without the address (khitab) as seen with the sleeper.
The practical consequence: a sleeper or unconscious person still has the obligation of prayer because time is the sign, even though they are not addressed.

وعن المجنون حتى يعقل أو يفيق فعلم منه أن الوجوب في حقهما مضاف إلى السبب ولا يمكن إضافة إلى الخطاب لعدم توجه الخطاب كذا في الحصول ١٢
Chapter: Legal rulings are connected to their causes. The obligation itself is upon the servant. (1) Established, i.e., the obligation of rulings in reality. Chapter: Legal rulings are connected to their causes; and that is because obligation is hidden from us, so there must be a sign by which the servant knows the obligation of the ruling. With this consideration, rulings are attributed to causes. Thus the cause of the obligation of prayer is time, evidenced by the fact that the address to perform prayer is not directed before the entry of time, but is only directed after the entry of time. (1) He said: [Connected...] That is because obligation is either by His command, and His command is known to us by revelation, but its renewed connections in aspects of demand across the passage of times are devoid thereof, for we do not know at which time the demand is connected. Hence there must be here marks, indicators, and signs for the specific aspects of demands. Since times are contexts for the renewal of His blessings and favor, and are informative for us, they are considered in general rulings as causes and reasons for their obligation, as in prayer and fasting. Thus the author undertook to explain the types of causes for legal rulings established by the Book, Sunnah, consensus, and analogy. By this it is known that causes here mean signs and identifiers that reveal rulings, not in the sense of effectors. The real cause for the obligation itself is the eternal command, which is the true act of God, and its place is taken by apparent causes like times. The real cause for the obligation of performance is the connection of the demand from the Lawgiver, and its apparent cause is the verbal imperative form. As in al-Husul. (2) That is because obligation is by God's command, and His command for prayer, for example, in the day or night, is not known when it occurs. If there were no causes established for it, knowing them would be difficult for servants. Then what is meant here by causes is reasons; because the cause in law is that which is a path to knowing the required, not that which necessitates it. But the mashayikh chose the word 'sabab' because it is more general. (3) He said: [Time] If you say: If time were a cause for the obligation of prayer, there would remain no existence for performance in it, because the cause is always prior to its effect. Then obligation would be delayed from time, losing the conditions of container and condition. I say: The cause is the part through which performance is separated, and it is prior to performance. Hence there is no necessary delay of obligation from time in the matter of one who begins prayer. As in al-Ma'din. (4) He said: [Before the entry of time, and it is indicated by His saying: Establish prayer at the decline of the sun until the darkness of the night (Isra 17:78). The principle in the lam is causality. If it is interpreted as time in view of His saying: So exalted is Allah when you enter the evening and when you enter the morning (Rum 30:17), it does not harm us, because timing does not negate causality based on what we have verified that it is the apparent cause and that it is originally a container. This statement of the author also indicates. Chapter: Legal rulings are connected to their causes, i.e., the address. And the address is a confirmatory for the obligation of performance and a definer for the servant of the cause of obligation before it. This is for the buyer, as we say to the husband. The pronoun is first object, second object. As we say: Pay the price of the sold item and pay the maintenance of the married woman. And there is no existing thing that the servant knows here except report, himself, the obligation itself, i.e., in prayer, the entry of time. So it is clarified that obligation is established by the entry of time. And because obligation is established upon the one over whom the address does not apply, like the sleeper and the unconscious person, and there is no obligation before time. The waw is for a circumstance. That time is only an identifier, and the cause is the direction of the address, based on the fact that the address is not directed before the entry of time. Thus the correlation indicates that it is a cause, but it is in the sense of identifier and sign, so it does no harm that you [argue] about coincidental causes. This is what our master Muhammad Hasan al-Sunbuli said in Hushiyat al-Hawashi. (1) He said: [And the address...] This is an answer to a question: Since the obligation itself is established by the cause which is time, what is the benefit of the address? He answered by saying: 'And the address is a confirmatory... etc.' As in al-Ma'din. (2) He said: [Before it] i.e., before the obligation of performance, and it is separate from the obligation itself, because the address is established by the command, and the obligation itself is established by the cause which is time. The cause is not the command, for the cause establishes the obligation itself, and the address with the command is directed after that cause and informs of the cause of the obligation which is time before it, i.e., before the obligation of performance. As in al-Ma'din. (3) He said: [Pay the price of the sold item and pay the maintenance of the married woman, for the price is obligatory by the sale and maintenance by marriage, and their performance is obligatory upon demand.] As we say: 'Pay' – by this it is known that obligation is established by the cause i.e., time prior to the obligation of performance by the address directed after time. Because the obligation i.e., the obligation of prayer is established in the right of one over whom the address does not apply, due to his being incapable of understanding the address, like the sleeper and the unconscious person, not exceeding a day and night, until he is commanded to make up after waking and recovery, and making up is only obligatory as a substitute for what was missed. Thus we know that obligation is established for both of them. Do you not see that the menstruating woman does not have to make up because performance was not obligatory upon her? As in al-Fusul. (4) 'Defines' is derived from 'definition', i.e., it identifies the obligation itself. The verbal clause is an attribute of his saying 'existing', and the 'except' is his saying 'except the entry of time'. (5) He said: [Like the sleeper... etc.] For both of them in this state are not fit for understanding the address, and attention is not directed except with understanding it. To this is indicated in His saying: Do not approach prayer while you are intoxicated until you know what you are saying (Nisa 4:43), and in the hadith of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her): 'The pen is lifted from three: from the sleeper until he wakes, from the child until he matures, and from the insane until he comes to his senses or recovers.' From this it is known that obligation for them is attributed to the cause and cannot be attributed to the address due to the lack of direction of the address. As in al-Husul.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the usul maxim that legal rulings (ahkam) are connected to their causes (asbab). The author, likely Imam al-Shashi, begins by stating the principle and then explains the rationale. The core idea is that obligation (wujub) is not directly perceptible; it is known through external signs (amarat). For example, the obligation of prayer is known through the entry of time. The commentary elaborates that the real cause of obligation is God's eternal command, but the apparent cause is the time itself, which serves as a sign (amara) and identifier (mu'arraf), not an efficient cause (mu'aththir). This distinction is crucial: in Hanafi usul, 'sabab' is a path to knowledge, not a necessitating agent. The passage then addresses potential objections: if time is the cause, why is the address (khitab) needed? The answer is that the address (the command 'Pray!') is a confirmatory (muthbit) for the obligation of performance (wajib al-ada) and a definer (mu'arraf) of the cause's prior existence. The commentary provides examples from transactions (sale price, marriage maintenance) to show the same pattern: the cause (like sale) establishes the obligation, and the demand (like 'pay!') is subsequent. It further argues that obligation exists even when the address is not heard, such as for a sleeping or unconscious person, who must make up missed prayers, proving that the obligation is due to the time, not the address. The passage also cites Quranic verses (17:78, 30:17) and hadith (the lifting of the pen) as proofs. The overall teaching is that legal obligations are determined by objective causes (like time) that are universally applicable, not by subjective awareness of commands.

Key Terms
السبب (al-sabab): An external sign (amara) that indicates the presence of a legal ruling; not an efficient cause.
الوجوب (al-wujub): The legal obligation of an action, belonging to the category of wajib, established by the Lawgiver.
الخطاب (al-khitab): The divine address or command (e.g., 'Pray!') that confirms the obligation of performance and informs the servant of the prior cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, what is the actual function of time in relation to the obligation of prayer?
A) Time is the efficient cause that creates the obligation.
B) Time is a sign (amara) that indicates the obligation and serves as a cause in a metaphorical sense.
C) Time is irrelevant; only the address (khitab) matters.
D) Time is a condition (shart) for the validity of prayer.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, sabab is an amara (sign) that reveals the ruling, not an efficient cause; this distinction affects rulings such as prayer obligation for the unconscious.

Definition and Divisions of al-Mani' (Preventer)
Definition and divisions of al-mani' with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-mani' in Hanafi usul and distinguish between the two divisions based on acceptance of takhsis al-illa.

Core Idea
Al-mani' prevents the ruling of the cause, but its classification shifts depending on whether one allows takhsis al-illa.

In this passage, the author introduces the concept of al-mani' (preventer) — a key tool in Hanafi legal reasoning. We will first grasp its core definition, then see how it divides into categories depending on a foundational dispute: whether a legal cause ('illa) can be 'specified' (takhsis) so that its ruling is blocked by a preventer. The two leading examples — a sale with an option clause and a person with a chronic excuse — illustrate how the same scenario is analyzed differently by the two groups.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of al-mani'
Locate the sentence: 'المانع: وهو ما يمنع ابتداء حكم العلة'. This is the core definition: the preventer is that which prevents the beginning of the ruling of the cause. 'Beginning' here indicates that the ruling never starts, rather than it being interrupted later.
Note the contrast with something that nullifies a ruling already in effect — that would be a different category.
2
Understand the first group's division (allowing takhsis)
The text then gives two examples: (1) sale with a condition of option, and (2) continuation of time in the case of a person with an excuse. Under the view that takhsis al-illa is permissible, these are cases where the preventer blocks the ruling from applying even though the cause is present. The first example prevents the immediate transfer of ownership; the second prevents the nullification of wudu by the hadath.
These examples correspond to the 'third' and 'fourth' types mentioned — the text implies two earlier types not quoted here.
3
Contrast with the second group's division (denying takhsis)
For those who do not allow takhsis, the preventer is divided into three types: one that prevents the beginning of the cause, one that prevents its completion, and one that prevents the continuation of the ruling. On this view, when the cause is fully complete, the ruling inevitably follows.
The key difference: the first group says 'preventer blocks the ruling'; the second says 'preventer blocks the completeness of the cause'.
4
Map the two schemes onto each other
Notice the author states: 'كل ما جعله الفريق الأوّل مانعا لثبوت الحكم جعله الفريق الثاني مانعا لتمام العلة' — everything the first group treats as a preventer of the ruling, the second group treats as a preventer of the completion of the cause. So it is a matter of relabeling the same preventer.
This sets up the dispute over takhsis, which is explored in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل أقسام الموانع
المانع: وهو ما يمنع ابتداء حكم العلة. ومثال الثالث: البيع بشرط الخيار وبقاء الوقت في حق صاحب العذر. ومثال الرابع: خيار البلوغ والعتق والرؤية وعدم الكفاءة والاندمال في باب الجراحات على هذا الأصل، وهذا على اعتبار جواز تخصيص العلة الشرعية، فأما على قول من لا يقول بجواز تخصيص العلة فالمانع عنده ثلاثة أقسام: مانع يمنع ابتداء العلّة ومانع يمنع تمامها ومانع يمنع دوام الحكم، وأمّا عند تمام العلة فيثبت الحكم لا محالة وعلى هذا كل ما جعله الفريق الأوّل مانعا لثبوت الحكم جعله الفريق الثاني مانعا لتمام العلة. وعلم منه أن تخصيص العلة هو تخلف الحكم عنها لمانع. وقد قال به الكرخي ومشايخ العراق وكثيرون وهو الظاهر قياساً على العلة المنصوصة، وفخر الإسلام ومن تبعه وكثيرون منعوا تخصيصها. قال الفاضل السنبهلي: هذا نزاع لفظي، فإن العلة إن اعتبرت علّة موجبة وأخذ رفع الموانع في جانب العلة فالتخصيص ممتنع، وإن اعتبرت العلة مقتضيةً ومستدعيةً لوجود المعلول وموجبةً للحكم على تقدير عدم الموانع فالتخصيص جائز.
Chapter on the divisions of preventers. The preventer: it is that which prevents the beginning of the ruling of the cause. And the third example: sale with a condition of option and the continuation of time in the case of the person with an excuse. And the fourth example: the option of puberty, emancipation, inspection, lack of suitability, and healing in the chapter of wounds on this principle. And this is based on considering the permissibility of specification of the legal cause. As for the view of one who does not permit specification of the cause, with him the preventer has three divisions: a preventer that prevents the beginning of the cause, a preventer that prevents its completion, and a preventer that prevents the continuation of the ruling. And when the cause is complete, the ruling is established inevitably. And on this basis, everything that the first group considered a preventer of the establishment of the ruling, the second group considered a preventer of the completion of the cause. And it is known from this that specification of the cause is the non-occurrence of the ruling due to a preventer. Al-Karkhi, the scholars of Iraq, and many others held it, and it is the apparent view by analogy to the explicit cause. Fakhr al-Islam and his followers and many others prevented its specification. Al-Fadil al-Sanbuhli said: This is a terminological dispute: if the cause is considered a necessitating cause and the removal of preventers is taken into the cause, specification is impossible; if the cause is considered a motivating factor and demanding the existence of the effect and causing the ruling on the condition of absence of preventers, then specification is permissible.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of al-mani' (preventer) in usul al-fiqh and explains two major divisions according to whether one accepts takhsis al-illa (specification of the cause). The text first defines al-mani' as that which prevents the beginning of the ruling of the cause. Two examples are given: (1) a sale with a condition of option prevents the immediate transfer of ownership even though offer and acceptance exist; (2) the continuation of the time of prayer in the case of a person with a chronic excuse prevents the nullification of wudu by a recurring hadath. Then, for those who do not allow takhsis al-illa, the preventer is divided into three types: preventing the beginning of the cause, preventing its completion, and preventing the continuation of the ruling. When the cause is complete, the ruling must follow. Thus, the difference is that the first group (who allow takhsis) treat the preventer as blocking the ruling, while the second group treat it as blocking the completeness of the cause. The passage concludes by noting that the dispute is largely terminological, as explained by al-Sanbuhli: if the cause is seen as necessitating (with removal of preventers part of its definition), takhsis is impossible; if it is seen as a mere motivator, takhsis is possible. This aligns with the Hanafi usul approach, which often allows takhsis al-illa by istihsan and other considerations.

Key Terms
المانع (al-mani'): That which prevents the beginning of the ruling of the cause (or prevents its continuation, depending on classification).
تخصيص العلة (takhsis al-illa): The non-occurrence of the ruling from the cause due to a preventer, despite the cause being present.
العلة (al-illa): The effective cause or ratio legis that necessitates a ruling when complete and without preventer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what defines al-mani' in the first group's view (those who allow takhsis al-illa)?
A) It prevents the beginning of the cause.
B) It prevents the beginning of the ruling of the cause.
C) It prevents the completion of the cause.
D) It prevents the continuation of the ruling.

Key Takeaway
The preventer (mani') is an external factor that blocks the ruling of a cause; its classification changes depending on whether one accepts takhsis al-illa.

The Dispute over Takhsis al-Illa: Terminological or Substantive?
Dispute over takhsis al-illa with resolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two positions on takhsis al-illa and apply al-Sanbuhli's terminological resolution to analyze a fiqh scenario.

Core Idea
Takhsis al-illa is a terminological dispute: whether the cause is necessitating or motivating determines if specification is possible.

This passage presents a key debate in Hanafi usul: can a legal cause ('illa) be 'specified', i.e., can its ruling be withheld by a preventer even when the cause is fully present? Two groups of Hanafi scholars differ. Al-Fadil al-Sanbuhli offers a resolution by noting that the dispute is terminological, hinging on whether the cause is considered a necessitator (mujiba) or a motivator (muqtadiya). We will walk through the two positions and their practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the issue of takhsis al-illa
The passage defines takhsis al-illa as 'تخلف الحكم عنها لمانع' — the non-occurrence of the ruling due to a preventer, despite the cause being present. The core question: can a cause be said to be present and valid yet not produce its ruling?
This is distinct from naskh (abrogation), which is permanent.
2
Present the pro-takhsis position (Hanafi Iraqi scholars)
Al-Karkhi, the scholars of Iraq, and many others hold that takhsis is permissible. Their evidence is qiyas on explicit causes ('illa mansusah), where specification is agreed upon (e.g., the cause of fasting in Ramadan is sighting the moon, yet the obligation is lifted for the traveler). This view is called 'al-zahir' (the apparent view).
This view allows the cause to be considered present but its effect blocked by a specific preventer.
3
Present the anti-takhsis position (Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi)
Fakhr al-Islam (al-Bazdawi) and his followers, as well as many others, disallow takhsis. Their argument is logical: if a cause is truly effective, its effect must follow; otherwise, it is not the true cause. For them, what the first group calls a preventer is actually a factor that makes the cause incomplete.
This view treats the cause as necessitating (mujiba) — when complete, the ruling inevitably follows.
4
Analyze al-Sanbuhli's terminological resolution
Al-Sanbuhli declares the dispute to be lafzi (terminological), not substantive. If one defines the cause as a necessitator (mujiba) that includes within itself the absence of preventers, then takhsis is impossible. If one defines it as a motivator (muqtadiya) that requires the absence of preventers to produce the ruling, then takhsis is possible. The difference is only in how we define the cause.
This resolution shows that both groups can agree on the fiqh outcomes; they differ only in their usul taxonomy.
5
Apply the resolution to a fiqh example
Consider a person who eats forgetfully while fasting. Those who allow takhsis say: the cause (eating) is present, but the ruling (invalidity of fast) is prevented by forgetfulness. Those who disallow takhsis say: the cause is incomplete because intentionality is part of the cause. Under al-Sanbuhli's resolution, both agree that the fast is valid; they only differ in how they describe the preventer's role.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf — the practical result is the same.

Source Text

Types of Obstacles to the Legal Cause
Defines three types of obstacles to the legal cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the three types of obstacles (mawani') in relation to the legal cause ('illa) and explain the Hanafi dispute on whether obstacles prevent the cause itself or its ruling.

Core Idea
An obstacle can block the cause itself or the ruling — Hanafis differ on which.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi presents a key classification of obstacles (mawani') in relation to the legal cause ('illa). The classification is tied to a major dispute among Hanafis: whether the 'illa can be specified (takhsis) — that is, whether an obstacle prevents the ruling while the cause remains complete, or whether it prevents the cause's completion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
According to those who do not allow specification (takhsis), obstacles are three: preventing the inception of the cause, preventing its completion, and preventing the continuity of the ruling.
2
Understand the opposing view
Those who allow specification regard any obstacle to the ruling as actually an obstacle to the completion of the cause itself. Thus, for them, only two types effectively exist: prevention of inception and prevention of completion.
3
Grasp the underlying principle
Both groups agree that when the cause is fully complete (with no obstacles), the ruling necessarily follows. The dispute is about what constitutes 'completion' — whether it includes the absence of obstacles or not.
4
Apply to a fiqh branch: fasting
Consider the case of eating forgetfully while fasting. Those who allow specification say the cause (intake) is present but the ruling (invalidity) is blocked by the obstacle of forgetfulness. Those who disallow say the cause is not complete because the act is excused by the Lawgiver, so the obstacle prevents completion.
5
Evaluate the nature of the dispute
The passage suggests the dispute is largely verbal (terminological), as the practical outcome may be the same. Recognize that the classification of obstacles depends on one's definition of the cause's 'completion'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل أقسام الموانع: فالمانع عنده ثلاثة أقسام: مانع يمنع ابتداء العلّة، ومانع يمنع تمامها، ومانع يمنع دوام الحكم. وأمّا عند تمام العلة فيثبت الحكم لا محالة. وعلى هذا كل ما جعله الفريق الأوّل مانعا لثبوت الحكم جعله الفريق الثاني مانعا لتمام العلة. وعلى هذا الأصل يدور الكلام بين الفريقين. وقد عرف في الفروع.
Chapter on the classifications of obstacles: The obstacle according to him (the one who does not allow specification) is of three types: an obstacle that prevents the beginning of the cause, an obstacle that prevents its completion, and an obstacle that prevents the continuity of the ruling. And when the cause is complete, the ruling is necessarily established. And upon this, whatever the first group made an obstacle to the establishment of the ruling, the second group made an obstacle to the completion of the cause. And upon this principle revolves the discussion between the two groups. And it is known in the branches.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi discusses the classification of obstacles (mawani') in relation to the legal cause ('illa). It presents the view of those who do not allow specification of the cause (takhsis al-'illa). According to them, obstacles are three: (1) preventing the inception of the cause, (2) preventing its perfection, (3) preventing the continuation of the ruling. For those who allow specification, an obstacle that prevents the ruling is considered as preventing the completion of the cause itself. This is the key point of the dispute. The passage then states that this principle is well known in the furu' (subsidiary rulings). The commentary by the muhashi (احسن الحواشی) notes that this dispute is largely verbal, as the cause can be viewed as either necessitating the ruling directly (then obstacles are part of the cause) or merely requiring it in the absence of obstacles (then specification is possible).

Key Terms
مانع (mani'): An obstacle that prevents a legal cause from producing its effect, either at the beginning, completion, or continuity.
علة ('illa): The legally effective attribute that necessitates a ruling.
تخصيص العلة (takhsis al-'illa): Specification of the legal cause, meaning that the cause may be present but its effect is blocked by an obstacle in some instances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to those who do not allow specification of the 'illa, how many types of obstacles (mawani') are there?
A) Two types: preventing inception and preventing completion.
B) Three types: preventing inception, preventing completion, and preventing the ruling's continuity.
C) Four types: adding preventing the cause's existence.
D) One type: preventing the ruling.

Key Takeaway
Obstacles (mawani') are classified into three types relative to the cause, and the dispute is whether they block the ruling or the cause's completion — a key usul distinction with fiqh applications.
